Raven's Garden

Chapter 1

CHAPTER 1

The raven landed on the chain-link fence just as the cop car turned the corner, its blue and red lights cutting the pre-dawn grey into jagged slices. It didn’t caw. Just watched, head cocked, one oil-slick eye fixed on Manny as he froze, one hand on the cold steel of the community garden gate.

He didn’t breathe. The soil under his nails, the scent of turned earth and wet cedar from the raised beds, the weight of the burlap sack holding six fat carrots and the last of the kale—it all went static. The cruiser rolled slow, a shark in shallow water. Its spotlight hit the fence, the raven, the hand-painted sign that read Sḵwx̱wú7mesh Úxwumixw Community Garden – Take What You Need, Leave Some for the Soil.

Manny’s grandmother would have said the raven was a messenger. Yéil. Trickster. Bringer of change. Right now, the message felt pretty fucking clear: get gone.

He melted back into the shadows of the tool shed, the burlap sack held tight against his hoodie. The spotlight swept over where he’d just been, lingered on the disturbed soil of Bed Four. The cop car idled. Manny could make out two silhouettes. One talking on the radio. The other, the passenger, just staring out at the garden. Not a patrol cop. The suit gave it away. The cut of it, even in the dim light, said city. Said money. Said trouble you couldn’t talk your way out of.

The raven hopped along the fence, closer to the car. It let out a single, harsh kraa that sounded like a laugh.

The passenger door opened.

A man got out. Tall, lean. He didn’t put on a hat. He just stood there, hands in the pockets of his overcoat, looking at the garden like he was assessing a property. He took a few steps, his dress shoes crunching on the gravel path. He stopped at Bed Four, knelt down. He picked up a clump of dark soil, rubbed it between his fingers. He looked toward the shed.

Manny’s heart was a fist pounding against his ribs. This wasn’t about the carrots. This wasn’t about the garden at all.

After a minute that stretched into an eternity, the man stood, brushed his hands clean on his coat. A deliberate, slow gesture. He said something to the driver, got back in the car. The cruiser pulled away, its lights dying as it turned onto East Hastings, swallowed by the city’s grumble.

Manny waited until the sound of the engine was completely gone, until the raven had taken flight with a rustle of powerful wings. Then he slipped out, latched the gate behind him, and moved. He didn’t run. Running drew eyes. He walked with purpose, head down, the sack over his shoulder, through the back alleys of the Downtown Eastside. The city was waking up around him in fits and starts: the rattle of a shop gate, the hiss of a bus, the low moan of a man still curled in a doorway. The air smelled of rain on pavement, stale beer, and the salt tang of the harbour.

He cut through Crab Park, where the tents formed a ragged city of their own against the backdrop of container cranes and North Shore mountains. The mountains were just beginning to catch the first pink of sunrise, their peaks sharp against the lightening sky. Sḵwx̱wú7mesh land. Always watching.

His destination was a three-story walk-up on Powell, sandwiched between a pawn shop and a place that sold industrial cleaning supplies. The building was old, its brick stained with decades of exhaust, but the windows were clean and there were planters on the fire escapes. The Willows. Not an SRO, not quite. A housing co-op his Auntie Dee had fought for, bled for, back in the 90s. A piece of ground that was supposed to be theirs.

He took the stairs two at a time to the third floor, the carrots bouncing in the sack. The door at the end of the hall was painted a deep forest green. A small, intricate carving of an orca was set into the wood at eye level. He knocked a pattern: two quick, one slow.

A series of locks disengaged. The door opened a crack, then wider.

“You’re late,” his sister said. Kelsey was seventeen, all sharp angles and sharper eyes, her hair in a long, dark braid over one shoulder. She wore an old band t-shirt and sweatpants. “I was about to send out a search party.”

“Got held up.” Manny stepped inside, the familiar scent of sage, fry bread, and old books wrapping around him. He dropped the sack on the small kitchen table. “Cops. At the garden.”

Kelsey’s eyes narrowed. “For what? Since when do they care about kale?”

“It wasn’t about the kale.” Manny pulled the carrots out, their earthy smell filling the space. “Guy in a suit. Got out, looked around. Like he was… appraising it.”

Auntie Dee emerged from the back room, a blanket around her shoulders. She moved slowly, the arthritis in her knees a constant companion, but her gaze was a laser. “Describe him.”

“Tall. White. Clean. Expensive coat. Acted like he owned the air he breathed.”

Dee nodded, her face a mask of weathered stone. She shuffled to the window, peered down at the street. “Lynch.”

“Who’s Lynch?” Kelsey asked, pulling a knife from the block to start trimming the carrot tops.

“Aidan Lynch. Works for the Mayor’s office. ‘Director of Strategic Development.’” Dee said the title like it was a curse. “He’s the smooth hand that holds the knife. Been sniffing around that garden for months. Calling it ‘underutilized civic space.’ Talking about ‘mixed-use potential.’”

Manny felt a cold knot form in his gut. The garden was more than a plot. It was a promise. A tiny, stubborn reclaiming. His mother had helped build the first beds, before she got sick. Before she was gone. “They can’t. It’s community land. It’s protected.”

Dee turned from the window, her smile thin and without warmth. “Nothing is protected, tákʷa̱. Not when there’s money to be made. Not when the city wants to pretty up this whole area for the rich people watching from their condos.” She gestured toward the gleaming towers of Coal Harbour, visible in the distance through the grimy window. “They see a problem to be cleaned up. We see our home.”

The kettle whistled. Dee made tea, her movements precise. She placed a mug in front of Manny. “You saw Yéil.”

It wasn’t a question. Manny nodded, sipping the bitter, herbal brew. “On the fence. It watched the whole thing.”

“Good,” Dee said. “Change is coming. Not the kind they’re selling in brochures. The real kind. The messy kind.” She fixed Manny with a look. “Your uncle’s back.”

The mug stopped halfway to Manny’s lips. “Ricky?”

“Got in last night. He’s down at the old rail yard. Wants to see you.”

Ricky. His mother’s brother. A face from a hazy childhood memory: laughter, the smell of diesel and saltwater, a pair of hands that could fix anything. Then, a different memory. Raised voices. A slammed door. His mother crying. Ricky disappearing into the city’s underbelly. He’d done time. For what, exactly, was a family mystery, whispered about but never clarified.

“What does he want?” Manny’s voice was flat.

“Says he has a proposition. Says it’s about keeping what’s ours.” Dee’s eyes held his. “You should hear him out.”

“He’s trouble, Auntie. The kind that leaves a stain.”

“We’re already stained, nephew,” she said softly. “The question is what pattern we make with it.”

Kelsey scraped the carrot tops into a compost bin. “I’ll go with you.”

“No,” Manny and Dee said in unison.

Manny stood. The adrenaline from the morning was gone, replaced by a heavy fatigue. But curiosity, that old snake, was uncoiling in his chest. Ricky was a link to his mother, to a past that felt more and more like a map to the present. And if Lynch was making moves on the garden…

“Where in the rail yard?” he asked.

Dee told him. Then she reached out, her hand gnarled but strong, and gripped his wrist. “Listen more than you talk. He’s family. But family can be a complicated current. Don’t let it pull you under.”

The old rail yard was a graveyard of industry on the edge of the port. Weeds pushed through cracked asphalt. Hulks of rusted machinery sat like forgotten monuments. The air was thick with the smell of creosote and low-tide mudflats.

Manny found Ricky in the lee of a faded yellow boxcar, its sides covered in vibrant, layered graffiti. A small, smokeless fire burned in a metal drum, a battered tin pot resting on its edge. Ricky was crouched, poking the fire with a stick. He looked up as Manny approached.

Time had carved deeper lines into his face, and his hair was shot through with grey, but the eyes were the same. Dark, quick, missing nothing. He wore a heavy work jacket and jeans scarred with grease.

“Manny,” Ricky said, not smiling, but his voice held a warmth that surprised Manny. “Look at you. All grown into your dad’s shoulders.”

“Ricky.” Manny stayed a few feet away. “Auntie Dee said you wanted to talk.”

“I do. Sit. Have some tea. It’s shit, but it’s hot.” Ricky poured a dark liquid into a tin cup and handed it over. Manny took it, sat on an overturned crate.

For a minute, they just listened to the fire crackle and the distant cry of gulls. The city felt miles away.

“I heard about the suit this morning,” Ricky said finally. “Lynch.”

“News travels.”

“It does in the right tunnels. He’s not acting alone. He’s the frontman for a consortium. Private developers, some overseas money, a couple of city councillors with their hands out. They’ve got a plan. They call it ‘The Harbourside Revitalization.’ Starting with that garden. Then the co-op. Then the whole strip. They want it all shiny and empty for the people who work in those glass towers.”

Manny’s knuckles were white around the tin cup. “We’ll fight it. We always have.”

Ricky shook his head. “Not this time. This isn’t a zoning meeting, nephew. This is a money tsunami. They’ve got the bylaws ready to change, the inspections lined up to fail you, the police ready to ‘keep order.’ They’ll offer buyouts so low it’s an insult. When people refuse, things will start to happen. Fires. Plumbing failures. ‘Safety concerns.’ They’ll make life impossible until you leave.”

“So what’s your proposition?” Manny asked, the bitterness leaking into his voice. “You gonna sell us life jackets?”

Ricky’s eyes flashed, but his voice stayed calm. “I’ve been in the belly of this city for a long time, Manny. I know how it eats. And I know where it keeps its secrets.” He leaned forward, the firelight dancing in his eyes. “Lynch has a boss. A man named Vorstad. He’s the real money. He’s also a collector. Not of art, but of… our art. Our history. He’s got a vault. In his house up in Point Grey. He’s got masks, rattles, bentwood boxes. Things that were taken. Things that belong to the people.”

Manny stared at him. “You want to rob him.”

“I want to reclaim,” Ricky corrected, his voice low and fierce. “But it’s not just about the things. It’s about the leverage. Vorstad is a ghost. He never gets his hands dirty. But his collection? It’s his pride. His dirty little secret of how he really made his first fortune. If certain photos of that collection found their way to the right people—the band councils, the media—his ‘philanthropist’ image goes up in smoke. The whole development deal gets… complicated.”

“And you need me for what? To be your conscience?”

“I need your eyes,” Ricky said. “Vorstad is having a big fundraiser at his house next week. For the mayor’s re-election campaign. They’ll be hiring local catering, local staff. I’ve got a guy who can get you in. You’re a smart kid. You see things. You learn the layout, the routines, the security. You’re just a pair of hands clearing plates. Invisible.” He paused. “Like you were this morning in the garden.”

Manny felt the world tilt. This was madness. Criminal. Dangerous. But the image of the suit, Lynch, kneeling in their garden soil, assessing it… The image of Kelsey and Auntie Dee being forced out of The Willows…

“Why me?” Manny whispered.

“Because you’ve got a stake in this,” Ricky said. “And because you’re your mother’s son. She had a fire in her. I see it in you, under all that careful quiet. She’d want you to fight, Manny. Not just with signs and petitions. With whatever tools the land puts in your hands.”

The wind shifted, blowing smoke from the drum into Manny’s face. For a second, it smelled not just of wood, but of sage, of the garden, of his mother’s hair. Yéil had brought a message, all right. It wasn’t a gentle one.

He looked at his uncle, at the fierce, desperate hope in his weathered face. He thought of the raven, watching, waiting to see what he would do.

“Tell me about the fundraiser,” Manny said.

Chapter 2

CHAPTER 2

The rain started as Manny walked back from the rail yard, a fine, persistent mist that beaded on his hoodie and blurred the streetlights into smears of gold. The city’s sounds were muffled, softened. His mind wasn’t. It churned, a riot of doubt and a strange, humming anticipation. Ricky’s words echoed. Your mother’s son. Whatever tools the land puts in your hands.

Was this a tool? Or a trap?

He found Kelsey not at home, but at the community centre two blocks over, in the computer lab. She was hunched over a terminal, her face lit by the blue glow of the screen. Images of architectural renderings, city council meeting minutes, and corporate registries were tiled across the display.

“Digging?” Manny said, pulling up a chair beside her.

She didn’t jump. Kelsey had a preternatural awareness of her surroundings. “You’re back. And you look like you saw a ghost. Or made a deal with one.”

“Ricky’s not a ghost. He’s too solid. More like a boulder in a river.” Manny nodded at the screen. “What’d you find?”

“Aidan Lynch,” she said, clicking on a PDF. “Salary: one hundred and eighty-five thousand a year. Member of the Vancouver Club, the Shaughnessy Golf and Country Club. Wife, two kids in private school. Clean record. Too clean.” She opened another tab. “But his brother-in-law owns a construction firm, Sanderson Builders. That firm has won every single ‘competitive bid’ for city-backed affordable housing projects in the last four years. Funny, that.”

“Follow the money,” Manny murmured.

“Always.” Kelsey’s fingers flew over the keyboard. “Now, ‘The Harbourside Revitalization’ is just a pretty name. The holding company behind it is registered in the Cayman Islands. A real dead end. But the local face is a development firm called ‘Corvus Holdings.’ Fancy office in Yaletown. CEO is a man named Elias Vorstad.”

The name hit Manny like a physical blow. Vorstad.

“Go on,” he said, his voice tight.

“Vorstad. Sixty-eight. German-Canadian. Made his initial money in resource extraction up north. Mining, mostly. Sold out at the right time, pivoted to ‘sustainable development’ and philanthropy. Big donor to the museum, the symphony, the mayor’s last campaign. A pillar of the community.” Kelsey’s lip curled. “He’s also the majority owner of Corvus Holdings. Which, according to a buried environmental assessment appeal from five years ago, was implicated in the destruction of a suspected ancestral site near Squamish to make way for a highway interchange. Settlement was reached out of court. Records sealed.”

“A collector,” Manny said, almost to himself.

Kelsey shot him a sharp look. “What?”

Manny made a decision. He couldn’t do this alone. He needed her eyes, her fierce, analytical mind. He lowered his voice, though the lab was empty save for an old man sleeping in a chair by the door. He told her. About Ricky’s proposition. About the vault. About the fundraiser.

Kelsey listened, her expression unreadable. When he finished, she turned back to the screen, silent for a full minute. Then she said, “It’s stupid. It’s reckless. It’s probably a felony.”

“Yeah.”

“It’s also the only move that doesn’t involve us just waiting to get rolled over.” She swiveled to face him. “Ricky’s right about one thing. They play a different game. We show up at council meetings with our signs, and they nod and take notes and then go to dinner with Vorstad and decide our fate over steak and Scotch. The rules are theirs. The board is theirs.”

“So we cheat?” Manny asked.

“We change the game,” Kelsey corrected. “But to do that, we need to see the whole board. Ricky’s offering a peek. A dangerous peek.” She leaned in. “You can’t do this alone. You need a second pair of eyes in that house. I can get in too.”

“How? You’re seventeen.”

“The catering company, ‘Peak Service,’ they hire temps for big events. Mostly through an agency. But they also take local referrals for ‘authentic cultural ambiance’—their words, not mine. Auntie Dee knows the woman who does the floral arrangements for these things. She’s Coast Salish. She could get me in as an assistant. Carrying arrangements, helping with setup. I’d be a ghost, just like you.”

Manny felt a surge of protective fear. “No. Absolutely not. If this goes wrong—”

“If this goes wrong, we’re all in the same sinking canoe, Manny,” she hissed. “You think they’ll just evict you and let me and Dee stay? This is family. This is our ground. I’m not a kid you need to keep on the shore.”

He saw his mother in her then. The same stubborn set of the jaw, the same fire in the dark eyes. He’d been trying to protect a memory, not the living, breathing, capable woman in front of him.

“You tell Auntie Dee about any of this, she’ll lock us both in the basement,” he said finally.

A ghost of a smile touched Kelsey’s lips. “Auntie Dee knows more than she lets on. She probably already knows about Ricky’s plan. She sent you to him, didn’t she?”

He hadn’t thought of it that way. Dee, the strategist, moving pieces on a board only she could see.

“Okay,” he said, the word feeling like a door swinging open into unknown, dark space. “We do it together. But we’re observers only. We see. We learn. We get out. The actual… reclamation… that’s Ricky’s play. We stay clean.”

Kelsey nodded, but her eyes were already far away, planning, calculating. “Observers. For now.”

Two days later, Manny stood in a cramped, steamy kitchen in a warehouse in East Vancouver, wearing a stiff white shirt and black pants that smelled of industrial laundry soap. The uniform of the invisible.

“You, new guy! Manny, right?” A harried woman with a clipboard and a headset barked at him. “Trays. You’re on passed hors d’oeuvres. Salmon tartare on brioche, beef tataki on sesame crisps, wild mushroom arancini. Don’t hover. Don’t offer to the same person twice. Don’t block the views. The view is the art, the architecture, and the other rich people. You are wallpaper. Got it?”

“Got it,” Manny said, hefting a heavy silver tray.

“Good. Door’s that way. The circus is in the big top.”

The “big top” was the main living area of Elias Vorstad’s Point Grey home. It wasn’t a house; it was a statement. A monument of glass, steel, and cantilevered concrete perched on the cliffs, all sharp angles and breathtaking views of the Strait of Georgia. The inside was a curated museum of cold elegance: minimalist furniture, a massive stone fireplace, and walls adorned with contemporary art that looked like explosions of colour and geometry.

And people. Dozens of them. The air hummed with the low, moneyed murmur of conversation, the clink of crystal, the scent of perfume and seared meat. Men in bespoke suits, women in dresses that cost more than a year’s rent at The Willows. Manny moved among them, a phantom. Salmon tartare? Beef tataki?

His eyes, however, were not on the guests. They were mapping. The floor plan. The location of doors—especially the ones that didn’t lead to kitchens or balconies. The security. He spotted two types: obvious, broad-shouldered men in dark suits with earpieces, standing at polite attention near the main entries; and subtler, a man posing as a sommelier, his eyes constantly scanning, and a woman in a chic dress who never seemed to be in a conversation, her posture too alert.

He saw Lynch. The man from the garden stood near the fireplace, holding a tumbler of amber liquid, laughing at something a silver-haired man was saying. He looked utterly in his element, a predator among predators.

Then, he saw Kelsey. She was across the vast room, wearing a simple black dress, helping a older Salish woman adjust a massive, stunning arrangement of twisted cedar boughs, red osier dogwood, and delicate white orchids that erupted from a carved clay vessel. She looked small, serious, and completely believable. Her eyes met his for a fraction of a second, then flickered meaningfully towards a wide, polished steel door set into a stone wall at the far end of the room. It had no visible handle, just a discreet keypad glowing faintly blue. The vault. It had to be.

Manny circulated, his tray growing lighter. He drifted towards the steel door, pretending to offer arancini to a group discussing yacht tariffs. Up close, the door was imposing. The keypad was a modern, sleek model. He noticed a tiny, almost imperceptible lens above it. A retinal scanner? He moved on, heart thudding.

He was near the floor-to-ceiling windows when a voice stopped him.

“The arancini. Are they truffled?”

Manny turned. The man was in his late sixties, with a lean, athletic frame and hair the colour of iron. He wore a navy blazer over a charcoal turtleneck. His face was lined, not with laughter, but with the calm, ruthless authority of a man who had spent a lifetime getting his way. His eyes were pale blue, like winter ice. They weren’t looking at the food on Manny’s tray. They were looking at Manny.

“I… believe so, sir,” Manny said, his server-mask firmly in place.

Elias Vorstad took one, nibbled it with a detached air. “Adequate.” He didn’t walk away. His gaze swept over Manny, lingering on his face. “You’re new with Peak Service.”

“Yes, sir. Just temping.”

“Hmm. You have an interesting look. Indigenous heritage?”

The question, so casually invasive, felt like a probe. “Yes, sir.”

“Coast Salish? Squamish?” Vorstad’s voice was conversational, but his eyes were assessing, cataloguing.

“My family is from the North Coast. Tsimshian.” It was a half-truth, a deflection Dee had taught him. Offer just enough.

“Ah. Beautiful art tradition. Strong lines. Powerful stories.” Vorstad smiled. It didn’t reach his eyes. “It’s important to preserve these cultures, don’t you think? To honour them.”

Manny’s skin crawled. The man was standing in his sixty-million-dollar glass fortress, talking about honour while his company planned to bulldoze a community garden. “Yes, sir.”

“We must be careful stewards,” Vorstad continued, as if lecturing. “Some things are too fragile for the modern world. They need protection. The right kind of protection.” He finally looked away, out at the dark, choppy water. “Carry on.”

Manny moved, the encounter leaving a cold film on his skin. Vorstad hadn’t been making conversation. He’d been taking a measurement. Of what, Manny wasn’t sure.

An hour later, his shift ending, Manny was in the steaming kitchen, returning his empty tray. He was about to head for the staff exit when he saw Kelsey slip through a different door, marked ‘Utility.’ He waited a beat, then followed.

He found her in a stark, concrete-lined corridor, lined with electrical panels and HVAC systems. The noise of the party was a distant murmur.

“The floral cooler is down here,” she whispered, her eyes wide. “I was putting leftovers away. Look.” She pointed to a junction box on the wall. Taped neatly beside it was a small, laminated schematic. A floor plan. For the alarm system. It showed zones. Zone 12 was labelled ‘Private Collection – West Wing. Motion & Contact.’

“It’s a separate system,” Kelsey breathed. “Dedicated to that room. Probably on its own circuit.”

Manny studied it. The vault door was one thing. But the room itself had its own protection. “We need to get this to Ricky.”

“Already did,” she said, pulling out her phone and showing him a clear, close-up photo of the schematic. “Sent it to a burner number he gave me.”

Before Manny could process her efficiency, a sound froze them both. Footsteps. Crisp, measured, coming down the corridor.

Kelsey shoved the phone into her bra. Manny grabbed a rag from a nearby cart and pretended to wipe his hands.

A security guard—the sommelier from earlier—rounded the corner. He stopped, his eyes narrowing. “Staff area is off-limits once your shift is over. You two lost?”

“Looking for the washroom,” Manny said, keeping his voice neutral. “Got turned around.”

The guard’s gaze flicked from Manny to Kelsey, lingering on her. “It’s back by the kitchen. This way. I’ll escort you.”

They followed him, Manny’s every sense screaming. As they passed the security guard, the man’s hand went to his earpiece. He listened, then said, “Copy. On my way.”

He picked up his pace, leaving them at the kitchen door. “Out. Now.”

They didn’t need telling twice. They collected their things from the cramped locker area and emerged into the cool, damp night. The valets were busy fetching Bentleys and Teslas. They walked, without speaking, for three blocks, until the mansion’s glow was behind them.

“He was suspicious,” Kelsey said finally.

“Yeah. But of what?” Manny replayed the guard’s look. It hadn’t been generic suspicion. It had been recognition. “Vorstad talked to me. Asked about my heritage.”

Kelsey stopped walking. “He what?”

“Made a point of it. Like he was… checking.”

“He knows who you are,” Kelsey said, her voice hollow with dread. “Not Manny the server. He knows you’re from the garden. From The Willows. Lynch would have told him. They have files, Manny. On everyone who’s a ‘problem.’” She hugged herself. “This wasn’t us observing them. This was them observing us.”

The mist turned to a light rain. The city around them felt suddenly darker, more watchful. They had gone looking for a weakness in the fortress. But as they stood on the empty, rain-slicked street, Manny felt the unsettling certainty that the fortress had looked right back.

Chapter 3

CHAPTER 3

The dream was always the same. His mother, kneeling in the garden soil, her hands dark with earth. She was laughing, saying something he couldn’t hear over the sound of the city. Then the soil would turn to ash, the plants would wither, and she would look up at him, her face etched with a sadness so deep it was a physical weight. You have to hold it, Manny, she would say, her voice clear now, cutting through the dream-noise. You have to hold the ground.

He woke with a start, the words echoing in the pre-dawn gloom of his room. The digital clock read 4:47 AM. He was sweating. The feeling from the fundraiser—the cold, exposed feeling of being seen by Vorstad—had seeped into his sleep.

He got up, splashed water on his face. In the main room, Auntie Dee was already awake, sitting in her armchair by the window, a cup of tea steaming in her hands. She was watching the first hints of light bleed into the sky over the harbour.

“You’re stirring early,” she said without turning.

“Couldn’t sleep.”

“The house of glass sits heavy on you.”

He shouldn’t have been surprised. He leaned against the doorframe. “You knew what Ricky would ask me.”

“I knew he would see the fire in you. I knew he would try to use it.” She finally looked at him. Her eyes were pools of dark, knowing calm. “The question is, are you letting him?”

“They know who I am, Auntie. Vorstad. He made a point of talking to me.”

Dee sipped her tea. “Of course he does. A good hunter knows the animals on his land. He was taking your scent. Measuring your fear.” She set the cup down. “But you were in his den. You saw his walls. What did you learn?”

Manny told her. About the steel door, the separate alarm zone, the schematic Kelsey found. About the security, both obvious and hidden. About Lynch, smooth and confident. About Vorstad’s cold, acquisitive eyes.

Dee listened, nodding slowly. When he finished, she said, “The vault is his heart. But a man like that, his heart is a fortress within a fortress. Ricky’s plan is a hammer. It will make noise. It might even crack the door. But it won’t open it.”

“Then what will?”

“A key,” she said simply. “Not a physical one. The right pressure, in the right place, at the right time. You need more than a schematic. You need a rhythm. The rhythm of the house. The rhythm of the guards. The rhythm of him.”

“We can’t go back in there. They’re alerted.”

“Not you,” Dee said. “There are other ways to listen to a house’s heartbeat.” She reached for a small, woven bag beside her chair and pulled out a folded piece of paper. It was a photocopy of a staff schedule. “The woman who does the flowers, Sarah. She’s my cousin’s daughter. She goes to that house every Tuesday and Friday for regular maintenance. Vorstad is a creature of habit. He works from home on Fridays. He has a standing massage appointment downtown at two PM, returns by four. The head of house security, the one who looked at you and Kelsey, his name is Carver. He takes a detailed logbook home with him every night. A man proud of his routines.”

Manny stared at the schedule, then at his auntie. The depth of her quiet intelligence, her network, her patience, humbled him. Ricky was a boulder, causing a splash. Dee was the current, wearing away stone over decades.

“You’ve been planning this,” he whispered.

“I’ve been watching,” she corrected. “A long game requires long sight. Ricky sees a single blow. I see a sequence. The fundraiser was the first move. Their noticing you was the second. Now we see their third.”

As if on cue, a heavy fist pounded on the apartment door.

Not the polite knock of a neighbour. This was authoritative, percussive. Boom. Boom. Boom.

Manny and Dee exchanged a glance. No fear in her eyes. Only a grim confirmation.

“The third move,” she said softly.

Manny went to the door, peered through the peephole. Two men in city-issued jackets stood in the hall. One held a clipboard. The other had the bored, impatient stance of a man used to delivering bad news.

He opened the door.

“Manuel Jacobs?” the one with the clipboard asked.

“Manny.”

“City Building Inspection. We’ve had a complaint regarding structural integrity and fire code violations in this building. We need to do a full walk-through. Now.”

“A complaint? From who?”

“That’s confidential. Now, are you going to let us in, or do we come back with a warrant and the fire department?”

Dee’s voice came from behind him, calm as still water. “Let them in, tákʷa̱. Let them see how we live.”

The inspectors came in. They didn’t look at Dee. They moved with a cold efficiency, tapping walls, checking outlets, peering at the ceiling with flashlights. They noted the age of the wiring, the lack of a second fire escape from their floor, the “improper storage” of Dee’s herbal supplies in glass jars on a shelf. They took photos. They wrote copiously on their clipboards.

“This window is painted shut,” one said, trying to open the living room window. “Major egress violation.”

“It’s been painted shut for thirty years,” Dee said. “Since before it was a co-op.”

“Doesn’t matter. It’s a violation. You’ll receive a formal notice. You have fourteen days to remedy these issues or the city will be forced to issue a vacate order for this unit due to imminent safety hazard.”

The words hung in the air, cold and final. The “third move” was not subtle. It was a bureaucratic bulldozer.

After they left, the apartment felt smaller, tainted. The home that had always been a sanctuary now had invisible cracks drawn all over it by men with clipboards.

Kelsey emerged from her room, her face pale. She’d heard everything. “They can’t do that.”

“They just did,” Manny said, anger a hot, tight coil in his chest.

“This is Lynch’s doing. Vorstad’s,” Kelsey spat. “They’re starting the squeeze.”

Dee picked up her tea, her hand steady. “The rhythm. They have increased the tempo. Now we must decide if we dance to it, or change the song.”

Manny’s phone buzzed. A text from an unknown number. Ricky’s burner.

Saw the city rats at your building. The squeeze is on. Time to stop watching and start moving. Meet me. Tonight. Same place.

He showed the phone to Kelsey and Dee.

“He’s right,” Kelsey said, her fear hardening into resolve. “They’re not giving us time. Fourteen days is nothing. We need that leverage now.”

Dee looked from her granddaughter to her nephew. She saw the decision forming, solidifying. She sighed, a sound of deep, weary acceptance. “The hammer it is, then. But you do not swing it blindly. You use what you know.” She pointed a bony finger at Manny. “The rhythm. Friday. Vorstad is out from two to four. Carver takes his logbook home Thursday night. The house’s heartbeat

Chapter 4

CHAPTER 4

The rain started as they left the apartment, a fine, persistent mist that beaded on Kelsey’s black rain jacket and made the sidewalk shine like the back of a wet seal. It was the kind of rain that didn’t fall so much as it hung in the air, a curtain you had to walk through. Manny felt it on his face, cold and needling. It matched the feeling in his gut—a low, constant prickle of dread. The city’s lights smeared in the damp, the neon of a bodega, the sterile white of a pharmacy window, all of it bleeding into the grey.

Ricky’s “same place” was a defunct arcade on Hastings, its windows boarded over with graffiti-tagged plywood. A faded sign, TIME ZONE, hung by one rusted bolt, creaking in the wet wind. Manny remembered it from years back, the cacophony of digital explosions and tinny music, the smell of stale popcorn and teenage sweat. Now it was just a dark mouth in the block.

Kelsey checked her phone, the screen’s glow making her face a pale moon in the alley’s shadow. “He says go around back. Service door.”

The alley was a canyon of dumpsters and fractured asphalt. The smell was old garbage and wet brick. A single bulb, caged and flickering, illuminated a steel door painted a grimy green. It opened before Manny could knock.

Ricky stood there, backlit by a single work lamp inside. He looked older than he had a week ago, the lines around his eyes deeper, carved by a new kind of tired. He didn’t speak, just jerked his head inward and stepped back.

They entered a space that was once a stockroom. The shelves were mostly empty, save for a few forgotten boxes of paper towels and industrial cleaner. The floor was concrete, stained. In the center of the room, the work lamp stood on an upturned milk crate, casting a stark, theatrical circle of light. In that circle sat a woman.

She was small, folded into a folding chair like a piece of origami. Her hair was a cloud of white, cut short and practical. She wore a thick, cable-knit sweater that seemed too heavy for her frame. And she was knitting, her hands moving with a swift, automatic rhythm, the needles clicking a soft, metallic counter-beat to the drip of water somewhere in the ceiling. But it was her skin that made Manny stop. It wasn’t just pale. It had a faint, pearlescent quality, a transparency, as if you were looking at her through a thin sheet of ice. He could see the vague, blue roadmap of veins in her hands, the suggestion of bone structure beneath the delicate skin of her face. She looked up, and her eyes were the colour of a winter sky, clear and impossibly sharp.

“Elara,” Ricky said, his voice uncharacteristically soft. “These are the ones I told you about.”

The woman—Elara—set her knitting in her lap. Her gaze swept over Kelsey, acknowledging, then settled on Manny. It wasn’t a look of appraisal. It was a look of recognition, as if she was checking a list only she could see.

“Kelsey,” she said, her voice a dry rustle, like leaves over stone. “Manny. Ricky says you’ve found a piece of the old song. And that the new conductors wish to silence it.” She gestured to a second crate. “Sit. The damp is less, here.”

They sat, the cold of the crate seeping through Manny’s jeans. Kelsey stayed tense, poised on the edge of hers. “Who are you?”

“A caretaker. A mender. Some called us Weavers, once.” Elara picked up her knitting again, the needles resuming their click-click-click. “This city… it is built on layers. Not just dirt and pipe and concrete. Layers of agreement. Of story. Of memory. My people, what’s left of us, we tend to the places where those layers wear thin. Where the… underneath starts to show through.”

Manny thought of the raven on the fence. The way the garden sign had seemed to pulse under the cop car’s light. Yéil. A messenger. “The artifact,” he said. “The thing from the site. What is it?”

Elara’s needles didn’t stop. “A Keystone. From a pattern laid down a long time ago, before the first mill was built on that shore. It holds a particular resonance. A truth the land remembers.” She looked at Manny again, that piercing winter gaze. “You felt it, didn’t you? Not just its weight. Its pull. Its hum.”

He nodded, unable to lie to those eyes. “Like a toothache in my bones.”

“Because you are sensitive to the fractures. You always have been. You just didn’t have a name for the static.” She said it as simply as stating the weather. “The man who wants to bury it, this Vorstad, he feels it too. But he feels it as a sickness. A wrongness. He is a man who believes in straight lines, poured concrete, quarterly reports. The Keystone’s song is a curve. A reminder. It undermines his certainty. So he must remove it. Bury it in steel and paperwork. Silence it.”

“It’s leverage,” Kelsey cut in, leaning forward. “Proof he’s hiding something. If we can get it, expose what it really is—”

“You will be called fools,” Elara interrupted, not unkindly. “Or liars. Or superstitious activists. He will have experts—his experts—call it a rock. A trinket. The narrative is his weapon, and he has more of it.” Her translucent hands worked the yarn, a deep, undyed grey. “You wish to fight a giant with a hammer. But you are swinging in the dark. You must first learn to see the cracks in his armour. The fault lines in his story.”

Ricky, who had been leaning against the wall in the shadows, spoke up. “Elara knows things. Ways in. She knows the rhythm of Vorstad’s house, his habits. Better than my binoculars.”

“The house has a heartbeat,” Manny said, repeating Dee’s words. “Friday. He’s out from two to four.”

Elara nodded. “His standing appointment at the Heritage Club. Massage and a steam. His driver takes him. The housekeeper leaves at one. The security system is a Vorstad-Lynch ‘Sentinel’ model. Top of the line. But all hearts have a murmur.” A faint, almost ghostly smile touched her lips. “There is a way into the house that does not trip the wires. A way that exists… between the layers.”

Kelsey shook her head. “A secret passage? In a modern mansion?”

“Not a passage. A thinness.” Elara set her knitting aside once more and stood. She seemed to unfold to a greater height than her frame suggested. “Come. I will show you. But only one of you.” Her eyes landed on Manny. “The one who hears the toothache must learn to see the cavity.”

Kelsey started to protest, but Ricky put a hand on her arm. “It’s the play, Kels. Trust it.”

Elara led Manny to the back of the stockroom, to a blank wall of painted cinderblock. A faded poster for a long-closed heavy metal band was taped there, the edges curled and brown. She placed her translucent hand flat against the wall, not on the poster, but beside it, on the bare block.

“The city is full of doors,” she whispered. “Most are just… forgotten. A path worn by memory, by need, by loss. They exist because at some point, the story of this place required a shortcut, an escape, a secret. And stories leave scars on the world.”

She began to hum. It was a low, tuneless sound, less a melody and more a vibration. Manny felt it in his molars. The air in the cramped room grew thick, charged, like before a lightning strike. The flickering of the work lamp seemed to slow, each pulse of light stretching longer.

Then, the wall wavered. It wasn’t an illusion. The solid, painted cinderblock blurred, its edges becoming indistinct, like a reflection in a pond disturbed by a stone. The poster of the screaming guitarist rippled and swam. A smell rose up—not damp or mildew, but the scent of ozone and cold stone, of deep, undisturbed earth.

“Reality is a weave,” Elara said, her voice echoing slightly, as if from the bottom of a well. “Most people only see the tight, finished front. Weavers see the loose threads on the backside. The places where the weave is worn, or was never finished properly.” She took his hand. Her skin was cool, dry as parchment. “Look through the pattern, Manny. Don’t look at the wall. Look at the space the wall forgot.”

He tried. He stared until his eyes watered, seeing only the blurring stone. He thought of the garden, the raven, the static. He thought of the Keystone’s deep, wrong hum in the Vorstad-Lynch vault. He thought of home, of the invisible cracks the inspectors had drawn, the threat of vacate orders and bureaucratic bulldozers. The anger and the fear coalesced, not into a thought, but into a feeling— a sharp, yearning need for a way through, a way out.

And then, he saw it.

It wasn’t a hole. It was a… seam. A vertical line of a different kind of dark, not shadow, but absence. It ran from floor to ceiling, a hairline fracture in everything. Through it, he caught a glimpse of moving air, of a different, cooler darkness.

“There,” Elara breathed. She stepped forward, pulling him gently. She didn’t push against the wall. She stepped into the seam.

And Manny followed.

The sensation was like being pulled through a sheet of cold, static water. There was a moment of profound pressure, a deafening silence that roared in his ears, and a disorienting sense of being turned inside out. Then it was over.

They were in a corridor. But it was like no corridor he had ever seen. The walls were rough, mortared stone, slick with condensation. The air was chill and still, carrying the mineral tang of underground water. A string of old, wire-caged bulbs ran along the ceiling, casting a dim, yellow light that created more shadows than it dispelled. The corridor stretched into darkness in both directions.

“Where are we?” Manny’s voice came out a whisper, swallowed by the dense silence.

“Beneath,” Elara said, starting to walk. Her soft-soled shoes made no sound on the damp stone floor. “The city’s bones. Some of these passages are old steam tunnels, maintenance runs. Others are… older. Forgotten footings, smuggler’s runs, places that were buried but never truly ceased to be.” She gestured with a faintly glowing hand. “This network is a fracture line. A place where the layers are stacked poorly. It is painful for me to walk in the world above for long. The noise, the harshness of the new patterns… it grates. Here, the weave is quieter. Softer. It is where I live.”

They walked for what felt like ten minutes, passing intersections of darkness, sometimes hearing the distant, muffled rumble of a subway train or the hollow drip-drip of water. Finally, Elara stopped before a section of wall that looked no different from any other. She pressed her hand against a specific stone, and with a soft grating sound, a section pivoted inward.

The space beyond was a sanctuary. It was a round chamber, perhaps twenty feet across, with a high, vaulted ceiling lost in shadow. The walls were lined with books—real books, leather and cloth-bound, interspersed with notebooks, scrolls, and strange objects: a collection of river-smoothed stones on a shelf, a mobile made of fossilized sea creatures, a loom strung with threads of copper, hair, and plant fibre. In the center of the room, a round rug covered the stone, and on it sat a low table holding a steaming teapot and two cups. The air was warm, smelling of dry herbs, stone, and a hint of beeswax.

An old man sat at the loom. He was even more translucent than Elara, a sketch of a person done in fog and moonlight. Manny could see the stone wall behind him through the curve of his spine. His hands, resting on the loom’s frame, were nearly invisible. He looked up as they entered, and his eyes were solid, dark, and deeply knowing.

“Kael,” Elara said. “A guest. The one with the Keystone’s echo.”

Kael did not speak. He simply looked at Manny, and in that look was an immense, weary patience. He gestured to a cushion on the floor opposite him.

Manny sat, feeling clumsy and solid in this place of whispers and half-seen things. Elara poured tea into a cup of rough, dark clay and pushed it toward him. The liquid was clear, with a single sprig of something green floating in it. It smelled of pine and something sharp, like crushed ant.

“Drink,” Elara said. “It will help you see. Truly see.”

Manny drank. It tasted of forest and frost, and a bitterness that made his tongue tingle. For a moment, nothing happened. Then, the room began to breathe.

It wasn’t the walls moving. It was the space within them. The air shimmered with faint, gossamer lines—like the ghost of a spider’s web, glowing with a soft, silver light. They connected everything: the books on the shelves, the stones, the loom, Elara, Kael. A vast, intricate, three-dimensional tapestry. And he saw that Kael was not just sitting at the loom; faint, fraying threads extended from his nearly vanished hands, connecting him to the larger web in the room, as if he was both weaver and woven.

“You perceive the structure,” Kael said. His voice was the sound of pages turning in an empty library. “The underlying pattern. This is what we tend. What Vorstad and his kind ignore, and in ignoring, damage.”

Manny looked down at his own hands. Faint, dull threads of muddy colour extended from his fingertips, connecting him to the floor, to the cup, to the air itself. They were thick, sluggish. “What am I seeing?”

“Connections,” Elara said, sitting beside Kael. “Responsibilities. Histories. The truth of relation. The Keystone’s song is a powerful node in this web. A point where many threads—of the land, the water, the old stories—are anchored. Vorstad’s development is not just concrete and steel. It is a story he is telling: a story of progress, of utility, of ownership. That story is like a hot knife, cutting through these threads. It causes pain. It creates… loose ends.” She pointed a faint finger at Manny’s chest. “Your thread to the Keystone is one. Your thread to your home, to your family, to that garden—these are others. They are under tension. They are being pulled toward a breaking point.”

The web of light pulsed gently. Manny saw, with a clarity that was terrifying, a knot of snarled, dark threads superimposed over the silver web in the direction he knew the Vorstad-Lynch site to be. A cancer of tangled, severed connections.

“You can show me a way into his house,” Manny said, the tea’s clarity sharpening his resolve into a cold, hard point. “To get the Keystone.”

Kael shook his head, a slow, painful movement. “The artifact is a symbol. Taking it may delay him. It will not stop him. He will simply tell a new story: vandalism, theft, the irrational actions of opponents. The true leverage is not the stone. It is the seeing.”

Elara leaned forward. “You must learn to see the fractures in his story. In his life. The hypocrisy, the hidden deal, the secret even his partners do not know. Every man who builds an empire on straight lines has a corner he had to cut, a foundation poured over something soft. You must find that softness. To do that, you must look with eyes that see the weave.”

“How?”

Kael reached out a hand that was more suggestion than substance. “The ritual of perception is not gentle. To open the inner eye is to feel every draft on the soul. You have a latent sensitivity. We can awaken it. But you will feel the city’s wounds. You will feel the pressure of the lies built into its streets. It will hurt.”

Manny thought of the vacate order. Of Dee’s weary acceptance. Of Kelsey’s fear hardening into a resolve as brittle as ice. He thought of fourteen days. He looked at the pulsing, beautiful, vulnerable web of light in the room, and then at the dark, snarled knot to the west.

“Do it,” he said.

The ritual was simple, and agonizing.

Kael had him lie on the round rug in the center of the chamber. Elara placed the river stones around him in a circle. She did not chant. She hummed again, that same bone-deep vibration, and Kael, with his ghostly hands, began to pluck at the air above Manny’s body.

It began as a tingling in his scalp, a sense of his skin being two sizes too small. Then the pressure came, not on his body, but inside his mind, behind his eyes. It was like a silent scream building in his skull. Visions, sharp and fragmented, flashed behind his eyelids:

—Not a raven, but dozens of them, a swirling black knot against a grey sky, and below them, the chain-link fence around the community garden being torn down by yellow machinery. —The living room window of his apartment, finally forced open from the outside by gloved hands, not to let air in, but to serve as an exit for his and Dee’s and Kelsey’s belongings, piled on the curb. —Marcus Vorstad, not in a boardroom, but in a dimly-lit room Manny somehow knew was in his house, holding not the Keystone, but a photograph of a woman with sad eyes, before locking it in a small, personal safe behind a painting. —The web of light, vast and city-wide, and in it, three brilliant, taut lines running from the dark knot of the construction site directly to… City Hall. And one of those lines thrummed with a familiar, corrupt rhythm: the rhythm of payoffs, of smoothed-over inspections, of favours called in.

The pain crested. It was the mother of all migraines, a spike of pure perception driven into the core of his being. He saw too much, felt too much: the grief in the stones of the old shore, the chemical anger in the soil of a vacant lot, the fleeting joy on a dark street corner, the deep, grinding stress of a thousand people trying to hold their lives together. It was a symphony of the city’s truth, and it was deafening.

He must have cried out. The world went white.

When he came back, he was on his side, shivering. The stones were gone. Elara was draping a rough wool blanket over him. The ghostly web was still visible, but fainter now, a permanent afterimage on his sight. The pain had receded to a dull, persistent ache behind his eyes, a new constant.

“You see,” Kael stated. He looked even fainter, as if the ritual had cost him.

Manny sat up slowly. The world was different. The stone chamber was itself, but overlaid with its shimmering structure of connection. He could feel the weight of the city above, not as mass, but as a cacophony of stories pressing down. And he could feel, like a compass needle pointing true north, the specific, rotten vibration of those three lines leading from Vorstad’s site to the heart of municipal power.

He knew things now. Not facts, but truths. He knew the hidden safe was more important than the corporate vault. He knew one of those three lines—the one that thrummed with the rhythm of dirty money—was the key. Find where it led, find the specific man, the specific favour, the specific lie.

“The house,” he said, his voice raw. “I can go in through the thinness. But I’m not looking for the Keystone.”

Elara’s winter-eyes gleamed with approval. “No. You are looking for the thread. The one he thinks is hidden. The one that connects his public story to his private corruption. Follow it. It will lead you to the hammer that can break him.”

Kael gestured weakly toward the chamber’s entrance. “The thinness in his house is in the wine cellar. Behind the 1982 Bordeaux. The rhythm is wrong there. A seam of haste in the foundation. You will feel it.”

Manny stood, the blanket falling away. He felt hollowed out and filled with fire. He saw the world’s scaffolding, and he saw the termites in the beams. He looked at Elara, at the fading Kael.

“Thank you.”

“Do not thank us,” Elara said. “We have given you a burden. Now go. Your fourteen days have become thirteen. And the man with the clipboard does not sleep.”

Manny retraced his steps through the stone corridor, the way now clear in his new sight, the path glowing with a faint, residual energy. He found the seam in the stockroom wall and stepped through, the static water sensation now familiar, almost comforting.

Kelsey was on her feet the moment he solidified in the arcade stockroom. “Jesus, Manny. You were gone twenty minutes. You just… vanished into the wall.” She searched his face. “What happened? You look like you’ve seen a ghost.”

He looked at her, and for a second, he saw the brilliant, fierce thread of her connection to him, to Dee, to their cause, a braided cord of vibrant gold and stubborn orange. He saw Ricky’s thread, a complex knot of loyalty and self-preservation and street-smart grey. He saw it all.

“Worse,” he said, the city’s pain a low throb in his temples. “I’ve seen the plumbing.” He picked up his jacket. “We’re not breaking into Vorstad’s vault. We’re breaking into his study. And we’re doing it Friday.”

“Why the study?” Ricky asked, pushing off the wall.

Manny headed for the door, the plan forming with crystalline, painful certainty in his newly awakened mind. “Because that’s where he keeps the receipts for the lies.” He paused, looking back at the blank wall where the seam had been. The echo of the weave was still there, a whisper of silver in the dark. “And I know exactly where to look.”

Chapter 5

CHAPTER 5

The map from the Keystone wasn’t lines on paper. It was a pressure behind Leo’s eyes, a gravitational pull in the pit of his stomach, tugging him east and down. It felt like remembering a dream about a toothache—the location was vague, but the throb was specific. He led Elara through the predawn streets, the city’s usual soundtrack of sirens and distant traffic replaced by a deeper, more troubling hum. He could hear the grid now, the whine of stressed cables, the groan of overburdened concrete. It was a symphony of strain.

“Here,” he said, stopping before a boarded-up service entrance in a graffiti-tagged alley. The sign, faded to a ghost, read DOWNTOWN CROSSCUT – AUTHORIZED PERSONNEL ONLY. The lock was a heavy, rusted thing. Elara didn’t ask how he knew. She just watched as he placed his palm against the cold steel door. He didn’t force it. He listened. He felt the lock’s internal geometry, the simple, stubborn truth of its tumblers. It was a lie of security. All he had to do was… disagree.

A soft click, muffled and final. The door swung inward on groaning hinges, exhaling a breath of damp, metallic air.

“Neat trick,” Elara murmured, but her voice was tight. She hefted her pack, her eyes scanning the alley’s mouth. “The map’s clear? First anchor point?”

“It’s a sinkhole,” Leo said, stepping into the darkness. “A place where the weave is thin, bleeding out. We plug it, the whole local grid stabilizes. Buys us time.”

“Buys who time, Leo?” she asked, following him, pulling the door mostly shut behind them. “You’re not just a custodian anymore. You’re holding the blueprints. That changes the job description.”

He didn’t answer. He flicked on his flashlight. The beam cut through the absolute black, revealing a narrow service tunnel, pipes crusted with mineral deposits snaking along the ceiling. The air was thick with the smell of stagnant water and ozone—the scent of a neglected wound. The pull led them down a flight of rusted metal stairs, each step ringing like a dull bell in the cavernous dark. The hum grew louder, resolving into a sound like a vast, distant engine missing a stroke.

They emerged onto a forgotten platform.

It was a cathedral of decay. Vaulted ceilings, once grand, were webbed with cracks, stalactites of concrete and rust dripping down. Tiled murals of the city’s founding fathers were blistered and peeled, the faces sloughing off into the dark. The tracks were black pools of water, reflecting the beam of Leo’s light in broken shards. But it was the air that was wrong. It shimmered, like heat haze off asphalt, but cold. In the center of the platform, the world seemed to sag, a barely perceptible funnel of distortion where the light bent and frayed.

“The anchor,” Elara said, her breath frosting. She set her pack down, pulling out a complex coil of copper wire and smooth, river-worn stones. “It’s almost gone. This isn’t natural erosion. This was chewed.”

Leo approached the distortion. The Keystone in his jacket pocket grew warm, then hot. The map in his mind zoomed in, showing him the rupture not as a hole, but as a tear with serrated edges. He knelt, reaching a hand toward the shimmering air. His fingers passed through a membrane of cold static, and he felt it—a sucking emptiness, a hunger. The city’s energy, the subtle life-force of its ley lines, was trickling out into a formless void. And beneath the hunger, a taste. Sharp, chemical, like burnt plastic and ambition.

“Someone’s been here,” he said, pulling his hand back. His fingertips were numb. “They didn’t just find this. They made it worse.”

Elara was already at work, laying the copper in a precise spiral around the distortion, placing stones at cardinal points. “Unravelers. Has to be. They don’t just want to profit from the cracks. They want to pull the whole tapestry apart so they can be the ones holding the needle afterward. Hurry. I need you to key the resonance. The Keystone has to sing to the old rock, remind it what it’s supposed to be.”

Leo moved to join her, the instructions flowing into his mind from the map—not words, but intuitions of frequency and form. He placed his hands on the central stone, cool and dense. He closed his eyes, reaching for the Keystone’s song.

That’s when the lights came on.

Not their flashlights. Great, glaring halogen beams mounted on tripods, flooding the platform with a harsh, surgical white. They clicked on in unison from the platform’s far end, from the arch of a maintenance tunnel, from the stairs they’d just descended. The shadows vanished, replaced by a flat, total exposure.

Leo and Elara froze, caught in the act.

Four figures stepped into the light. They wore no uniform, just dark, practical clothing, but they moved with a synchronized, predatory grace. Their faces were sharp, intelligent, utterly devoid of curiosity. They were not surprised. They were waiting. The man in front held a device that looked like a geiger counter crossed with a tuning fork, its needle twitching wildly, pointed directly at Leo’s chest.

“The Keystone-Bearer,” he said. His voice was pleasant, neutral. It was the most frightening sound Leo had ever heard. “And his loyal mechanic. We calculated a seventy-three percent chance you’d attempt triage on this primary bleed. Sentimentality for the old structures. It makes you predictable.”

Elara was on her feet, a short, weighted rod sliding from her sleeve into her hand. “Unravelers. You’re too late. The binding’s already started.”

The man smiled. It didn’t touch his eyes. “We’re not here to stop your binding. We’re here to harvest it. A forced stabilization creates a tremendous, localized spike in metaphysical tension. Like compressing a spring.” He gestured with his device. “We’ll catch the rebound. Use the energy to unstitch a much larger piece of the map. Starting with this entire city block.”

Leo understood. They weren’t vandals. They were engineers. They wanted to use his repair as a controlled demolition. The corruption wasn’t in the desire for power; it was in the clean, logical efficiency of its theft.

“You can’t hold that kind of energy,” Leo said, stalling, his mind racing over the map. The threads here were already frayed. The rebound could tear the whole neighborhood’s reality loose.

“We’ve refined our containers,” said a woman, stepping forward. She held a glass orb, inside of which a miniature, silver vortex slowly spun. “Your predecessor’s work was… messy. Ours will be elegant. A silent subtraction.”

They fanned out. No weapons drawn. They didn’t need them. Their tools were for the fabric of the place itself. One raised a hand toward the copper spiral Elara had laid. He made a gentle plucking motion. The copper wire vibrated, then snapped, whipping back with a sound like a gunshot. The river stones cracked, one after another, in rapid succession.

The distortion in the platform’s center pulsed, widening. A low groan emanated from the ancient concrete, dust sifting down from the ceiling.

“Now,” the lead man said.

The Unravelers moved as one. Two focused on Elara, their gestures complex and swift. The air around her thickened, becoming syrupy, slowing her defensive strike to a crawl. The other two advanced on Leo, their devices now emitting a high-pitched whine that drilled into his teeth. He felt a pulling sensation, not on his body, but on the connection between him and the Keystone. They were trying to sever it, to neatly extract the artifact from his soul’s keeping.

Panic was a luxury. The map flared in his mind. He saw the platform not as a solid thing, but as a collection of threads—load-bearing truths, historical memories, lines of force. The Unravelers were targeting the threads of structure, the ones that said this platform is solid, this platform holds.

He couldn’t fight them on their terms. He couldn’t protect every thread.

So he chose one.

He dropped to his knees, not in surrender, but to slam his palms onto the grimy tile of the platform. He bypassed the tearing, screaming threads of the present. He reached deeper, past the neglect, past the decay, to the oldest truth he could find. The memory of the bedrock. The intention of the first architects. The simple, profound vow of support.

He poured the Keystone’s energy not into reinforcement, but into remembrance.

You are a floor. You hold weight.

The effect was not a shield. It was a reassertion.

The section of platform between him and the advancing Unravelers didn’t strengthen. It remembered. The crumbling edges solidified, the cracks sealing themselves with a sound like grinding teeth. The concrete lost its sickly, frayed aura and gained a dull, immutable gravitas. It became, irrevocably and unquestionably, solid ground.

The lead Unraveler, stepping forward to make his final, extracting gesture, stumbled. His foot came down on what was now not just a platform, but a foundational truth. His device’s whine stuttered. The pulling sensation on Leo ceased.

The man looked down, then at Leo, his neutral mask finally cracking with a flicker of incredulous rage. “You idiot. You’re reinforcing the wrong layer! You’re anchoring it to the past! That’s a dead end!”

“It’s a floor,” Leo gasped, the effort of the declaration leaving him dizzy. “It does one thing. It holds.”

The other Unraveler targeting him gestured sharply, trying to unravel this new, stubborn truth. But you can’t unravel a fact. You can only break it. The man switched tactics, aiming his device at the ceiling above Leo. If he couldn’t take the Keystone, he would bury it.

The groan overhead became a shriek. A network of cracks raced across the vaulted ceiling. A massive chunk of concrete, rebar snarled like broken bones, broke loose.

“Leo!” Elara’s voice, strained against the thickening field.

He saw it falling. A truth of a different kind: gravity, mass, impact. The map showed him no clever thread to pull. He had anchored the platform to a truth of stability, and now a truth of destruction was about to test it.

Instinct took over. Not the map’s knowledge, but something older. The Keystone was a tool for seeing and mending the weave. But in that moment, Leo didn’t want to mend. He wanted to command.

He thrust his hand upward, not at the falling slab, but at the space it occupied. He didn’t try to reinforce the air. He imposed a new rule. A local, temporary law.

This is not a place where things fall.

The Keystone burned, not with its usual resonant warmth, but with a searing, white-hot arrogance.

The falling concrete slab struck the invisible field two feet above his head. It didn’t shatter. It didn’t bounce. It just… stopped. It hung in the air, defying every law, a stark, impossible sculpture. Dust poofed from its edges. The rebar quivered.

Silence, absolute and profound, swallowed the platform.

The Unravelers stared. The lead man’s device dropped from his limp fingers, clattering on the now-impossibly-solid floor. His look of rage was gone, replaced by naked, hungry awe.

Leo held it. The strain was immense. It felt like holding up the sky with a single, screaming muscle in his mind. He wasn’t mending. He was dictating. And it felt good. It felt like power, clean and absolute.

“Leo,” Elara whispered, her bonds slackening as the Unravelers’ focus broke. “Let it go. You have to let it go.”

He heard her from a distance. The slab was heavy, but the power was sweet. He could make this whole place obey. He could make the Unravelers forget how to breathe. He could rewrite the station, make it new, make it his.

The corruption wasn’t in the Unravelers’ logic. It was here, in this seductive, effortless rewrite. Why mend a broken thread when you could simply declare it unbroken?

The lead Unraveler found his voice. “Yes,” he breathed, his eyes wide. “Yes. You see it now, don’t you? The mending is for servants. The making is for kings. Join us. We don’t want to destroy the Keystone. We want to use it. Properly.”

The words slithered into the part of Leo that was tired of being a custodian, tired of back-alley fixes and desperate triage. He could be an architect. A god of small, perfect places.

Then he looked at Elara. He saw the fear in her eyes. Not fear of the Unravelers, or the collapsing ceiling. Fear of him. Of the cold, focused light in his own face she was seeing for the first time.

That look was a mirror. And in it, he didn’t see a king. He saw a man about to break the first, most important thing: himself.

He let go.

Not of the power, but of the command.

The law he had written snapped. Gravity reasserted its ancient claim.

The concrete slab dropped.

But Leo was already moving, shoving Elara backward. He didn’t try to stop the truth. He used it. As the slab smashed into the platform where he’d just knelt with a cataclysmic roar, he harnessed the explosive force of that reassertion—the shockwave of reality snapping back into place.

It tore through the Unravelers’ careful formations, knocking them off their feet. It ripped through the lingering, syrupy fields. It hit the central distortion, the sinkhole they had come to mend.

And it acted like a defibrillator.

The shock of pure, unadulterated real force jolted the frayed anchor point. The distortion didn’t heal. It slammed shut. The sound was a deep, tectonic thud that vibrated up through Leo’s bones. The cold, sucking emptiness vanished, replaced by a stunned, solid stillness.

The platform was whole. Not because he’d mended it with care, but because he’d shocked it into coherence with a violence of returning truth.

Dust filled the air, thick and choking. Halogen lights, knocked askew, cast crazy, swinging shadows. The Unravelers were scrambling up, coughing, but their coordination was broken. The lead man stared at the now-solid center of the platform, then at Leo, his awe curdling into something darker.

“You… you brute. You have a scalpel and you use it as a hammer.”

Leo tasted blood and dust. The Keystone was a cold, dead weight in his pocket. He felt hollowed out, ashamed. He had touched a pure, corrupting power, and a part of him still longed for its terrible simplicity.

“Go,” Elara said, grabbing his arm. Her grip was iron. “Now! The backlash is coming!”

She was right. The map in his mind screamed a warning. The forced, violent closure had created a pressure wave in the ley lines. It was rolling back toward them, a tsunami of distorted energy. The Unravelers’ glass containers wouldn’t hold it. Nothing here would.

The Unravelers saw it too on their devices, their readings spiking into the red. They turned and fled, not toward the stairs, but into a darker archway, one of them hastily activating a device that peeled a temporary, shimmering rift in the air.

Elara dragged Leo toward the opposite side of the platform, to a rusted maintenance door marked DANGER – NO ADMITTANCE. “Not our way out! Theirs! It’s the only path not in the wave’s direct path!”

“Their rift?” “It goes somewhere else! Better than being dissolved into raw possibility!”

They reached the fading rift just as the Unravelers’ last figure vanished through it. The air around it crackled, unstable, already collapsing. From behind them, Leo heard it—not a sound, but a sensation of the world taking a deep, tearing breath before the scream.

He didn’t think. He pushed Elara through the shimmering tear and threw himself after her.

The static water sensation was different this time. Not cool, but scalding. Not a passage, but a violation. He was being pushed through a hole that didn’t want him, into a place that wasn’t meant for visitors.

He tumbled out onto soft, damp ground. The air left his lungs in a rush. He rolled, coming up onto his elbows, retching.

The silence was the first thing he noticed. Not the silence of absence, but the silence of a held breath. Then the light. It was grey, diffuse, coming from everywhere and nowhere. He looked up.

They were in a forest. But it was a forest of mirrors.

Every tree—tall, slender, bone-white trunks devoid of bark—was sheathed in panels of flawless, silvered glass from root to canopy. The ground was a carpet of crystalline moss that glittered with internal light. The leaves overhead were not leaves, but shards of polished obsidian, reflecting the grey void above in infinite, fragmented copies. It was breathtakingly beautiful and utterly alien. His own reflection, wide-eyed and smeared with dirt, stared back at him from a dozen trunks, a thousand leaves.

Elara was already on her feet, her weapon drawn, turning in a slow circle. “Oh, no.”

“Where are we?” Leo’s voice was swallowed by the dense, non-sound.

“The Nexus. Or… the first antechamber of it. A reflection pool for deeper layers. It’s a neutral space. Or it was supposed to be.” She pointed.

Through the lattice of mirror-trees, Leo saw the Unravelers. They were fifty yards away, also disoriented, staring at their own multiplied reflections. But they were recovering quickly. The lead man was adjusting his device, scanning the environment. His gaze swept through the trees, and for a moment, his eyes met Leo’s—not directly, but in the reflection of a trunk between them.

In the mirror, the man’s lips curved. He raised a hand, not in a threat, but in a slow, deliberate wave. A greeting between fellow travelers in a strange land.

Then he turned and led his team deeper into the glasswood, their forms splintering and multiplying as they walked, until they were a thousand dissolving figures moving into the heart of the reflection.

Leo looked down at his own hands. In the crystalline moss, he saw his face repeated, pale and grim. He had used the Keystone to break a fundamental rule. He had felt the addiction of that power. And he had arrived, with his enemies, in a place where the only law seemed to be reflection.

What he had done on the platform would be reflected here. What he had almost become would be reflected here.

The man with the clipboard did not sleep. And now, Leo understood, he didn’t need to. Leo had just shown him exactly what kind of tool the Keystone could be in the wrong hands. And he had shown himself, too.

The hook for the next chapter was not a external threat. It was the slow, cold realization in his own mirrored eyes. The corruption wasn’t out there. It was in here. And he had just opened the door for it.

Chapter 6

CHAPTER 6

The glasswood held its breath. No wind stirred the silver leaves. No insect hummed. Only the faint, dry rustle of their own movements, a sound that seemed to come from every direction at once, echoed and mocked by the perfect mirrors of the trees. Leo took a step. A hundred Leos took a step. The crystalline moss crunched under his boot, a noise like breaking sugar, and a hundred crunching sounds layered over each other, a fraction out of sync, until the air buzzed with the ghost of the step he’d just taken.

Elara moved like a knife through water, her steps deliberate, her eyes never resting on any one reflection for long. “Don’t look at them,” she whispered. The whisper became a chorus. Don’t look at them look at them look at them.

“At what?” Leo’s own voice, multiplied, unnerved him. It sounded younger. More afraid.

“At the copies. They’re not just images. In here, perception has weight. Stare at a reflection too long, and it starts to stare back. It starts to… borrow.”

She stopped beside a trunk wider than a car, its surface a flawless mercury pool. In it, her reflection adjusted the grip on her weapon, but a half-second after she did. Leo blinked, and his thousand selves blinked back, a stuttering wave of motion through the grove. He focused on the physical ground—the moss, the faint, root-like veins of cobalt light that pulsed beneath it. The real. He had to hold onto the real.

A path, or the suggestion of one, wound ahead. It was a slight parting of the trees, a corridor of slightly shorter, sapphire-tinged mirror-bark. It looked deliberate. It also looked exactly like a hundred other partings he could see reflected and re-reflected in the labyrinth.

“That one,” he said, pointing.

Elara didn’t look at his hand. She looked at the ground where his shadow should have been. There was none. Instead, a faint, inverted halo of light seeped from the soles of his boots. “Maybe. Or it’s a trap laid by our own eyes. This place shows you what you expect to see. You expect a path, so it gives you a dozen.”

They started forward, choosing the original of the path he’d seen. Within ten yards, the corridor forked, each new avenue a perfect replica of the first. Leo glanced left. His reflection in a nearby tree glanced right. A cold trickle of sweat traced his spine. That wasn’t me.

“Elara.”

“I saw it.”

They chose the right fork. The air grew colder. Their breath began to fog, and the fog hung in the air, caught in the matrices of reflection, becoming a permanent, milky scar in the glasswood. Leo’s head throbbed with a low, optical pressure. He kept seeing movements at the edges of his vision—a flicker of his own jacket, the turn of Elara’s head—but when he snapped his gaze, there was only the stillness, and the slow, smug adjustment of his reflections to his new posture.

He was being edited. Frame by frame.

After what felt like an hour of silent, spiraling navigation, they reached a small clearing. In the center stood a single, black-barked tree, its mirror surface dark as a starless night. It did not reflect. It absorbed. Around it, the ground was littered not with moss, but with shards of broken mirror, each one showing a frozen, fragmented sliver of their arrival—Leo’s hand on the Keystone, the Unraveler’s wave, Elara’s drawn weapon. It was a crime scene of perception.

And sitting with his back against the dark tree, whittling a piece of what looked like solid light with a knife made of obsidian, was a man.

He was maybe fifty, maybe seventy; time seemed to slide off his features like water off glass. His hair was the color of tarnished silver, tied back, and his clothes were a patchwork of muted fabrics, stitched with threads that glimmered with a faint, internal glow. He didn’t look up as they entered. He blew on the shaving of light, which dissolved into motes of gold dust.

Elara’s weapon, which had never fully lowered, came up an inch. “Finn.”

The man—Finn—nodded. His eyes, when he lifted them, were the pale grey of old mirror-backing. They held a lifetime of watching. “Elara. Took you long enough. And you brought a guest.” His gaze settled on Leo. It wasn’t hostile. It was appraising, the way a carpenter eyes a piece of warped wood. “He’s vibrating. Like a plucked string. That’s the Keystone, isn’t it? You used it to get here. A hammer blow where a needle-thread was required.”

“We didn’t have a choice,” Elara said, her voice tight.

“There’s always a choice,” Finn said softly, returning to his whittling. “You just didn’t like the other options.” He gestured with the obsidian blade around the clearing. “Welcome to the Anchor Point. The only true thing in this forest of lies. This tree…” He patted the black bark. “It’s a drain. A sinkhole for misperception. All the stolen glances, the paranoid doubles, the identity-echoes that break loose… they eventually drift here and get swallowed up. I maintain it. I prune the corruption.” He held up the sliver of light. “This is a good day. A coherent memory. Most of what washes up is fear. Or vanity.”

Leo found his voice. “You’ve been here? For years?”

“Time is a necklace of mirrors in here, boy. Each one reflecting a different moment. I’ve been here for a long reflection.” Finn finally set his work down and stood. He was taller than he seemed, lean and corded. “The Unravelers. The ones who came through with you. They’re not lost. Moros has been here before. He knows how to navigate the ego-maze.”

“Moros?” Leo asked.

“Their leader. The man with the quiet eyes and the loud machine. He’s an archaeologist of collapse. He doesn’t just want to unweave the Nexus. He wants to study the process. To savor it.” Finn walked to the edge of the shards, careful not to step on them. “He’s heading for the Heartwood. The central mirror. If he reaches it, he can plant his device there, and the reflection will propagate through every connected layer. A cascade of unmaking. Starting here, in the place that defines reality’s shape.”

“Then we stop him,” Leo said. The words felt childish in the silent wood.

Finn’s old-mirror eyes fixed on him. “We? You are a door that’s been kicked open. You carry the tool he needs on your chest. And you are, if you’ll pardon the observation, crackling with the after-echo of a terrible power. Why would I ally with you?”

Leo had no answer. The cold realization from the platform returned, magnified by the countless cold faces in the trees. He had shown Moros the way. He had felt the seductive, totalizing clarity of the Keystone’s power—the way it made complex problems simple matters of force. Break the rule. Tear the fabric. It had felt like strength. Here, in this place of endless consequence, it felt like a sickness.

Elara stepped forward. “Because he’s also the grandson of Alistair Vale.”

The name changed the air in the clearing. The very light seemed to dim, then refocus. Finn went very still. For the first time, his composed, watchful mask slipped, revealing a raw, old wound beneath. “Alistair,” he breathed. The word was a eulogy. He looked at Leo anew, searching his face. “The jawline. The stupid, stubborn set of the eyebrows. God. He’d hate what you’ve done.”

“You knew him?” Leo’s heart hammered against the Keystone’s cold metal.

“He was my brother. Not by blood. By choice. By battle.” Finn’s voice was gravel. “He was the greatest Weaver of our age. He understood that power wasn’t about building walls, but about understanding the tension in the threads. Balance. Not force.” He pointed a finger at Leo’s chest. “He died protecting that thing from men like Moros. He hid it in the dead-layer, where no living mind could ever think to reach it, at the cost of his own life. And you… you use it to break a transit rule.”

The shame was a physical heat. Leo looked away, but his reflections forced him to meet his own eyes. “I didn’t know.”

“That,” Finn said, “is the problem with legacy. It’s handed to children.” He sighed, a sound that seemed to get lost in the trees. “But you are his blood. And the forest is reacting to you. Not just to the Keystone. To you. The reflections… they’re more active around you. It’s not just showing you what you expect. It’s showing you what you are.
