Escalation observed: subject’s emotional outburst during a traffic altercation caused simultaneous brake-line failures in three vehicles within a 15-meter radius, resulting in a non-fatal pile-up. Unsanctioned. High volatility. Outcome: Terminal. Contained. Family narrative: workplace accident.

The address was a warehouse district in the city’s north end. The containment was dated four days ago.

Leo went that night. It was a concrete-and-steel landscape, puddled with oily water reflecting the sodium glare of security lights. The specific warehouse was locked, a heavy chain on the roll-up door. But the Weavers were thorough. They wouldn’t just leave a body. They’d leave a story. A scene.

He found it around the back, near the loading docks. A section of metal siding looked newer, its bolts shiny against the rust. A “condemned” notice was plastered across a personnel door, citing “structural instability.” It was the sanitized stage dressing for a containment.

But they’d missed a spot.

Under a rusted dumpster, half-submerged in a oily puddle, was a small, singed object. Leo fished it out with a stick. It was a child’s toy. A little robot, made of molded plastic, one of its arms melted into a smooth, glossy blob. The heat damage was localized, intense. Not from a fire. From a sudden, catastrophic energy surge. A blowout.

Felix Kaur had been a father. This had been in his pocket, or his lunchbox. A talisman. When the Weavers came to “contain” him, his fear, his anger, his last surge of sympathetic resonance had focused on this tiny piece of plastic, the last object connected to something he loved. He’d fried it.

Leo held the ruined toy. It was cold. This was the cost of the Weaver’s way. A man dead. A family lied to. A child’s toy, melted by a father’s final, terrified pulse of a power he never wanted.

And this was the cost of Moros’s way? The laughing street, gone. The drowning poet, silenced. But were those losses greater than this? Was a city’s stolen chuckle worth a man’s life? Was a canal’s poem worth a child’s grief?

There was no equation. That was the lie of both ledgers. They presented the world as a balance sheet. But it was just blood and silence on one side, and blood and strange beauty on the other.

He pocketed the melted robot. The weight of it was different from the ledgers. It was specific. It was a father. It was a son. It was a failure.

He walked back through the warehouse district, the sky beginning to lighten to a dirty grey. He knew what he had to do. Not because Moros was right. But because the vacuum was wrong. Someone had to tend the garden. And if the only tools left were a madman’s scalpel and a bureaucrat’s shears, then you had to take one and learn how to prune.

He went to the dead-drop, a specific loose brick in the wall of a derelict laundromat two blocks from the garden. With a piece of chalk from the shed, he made the mark Moros had specified: a small, stylized raven, one wing extended. The call to arms.

He walked away, the red ledger under one arm, the black one under the other, the melted robot a secret, third weight in his coat pocket. He was a man walking between histories, toward a war with two fronts. He would use Moros’s indictment. He would use the Weaver’s own records. He would play the crusader and the cleaner. He would become the thing both sides feared: a man who had seen the books, and decided to write a different ending.

As he turned onto his own street, the first true light of dawn bleeding over the rooftops, a raven landed on a fire escape above him. It didn’t caw. Just watched, head cocked, one oil-slick eye fixed on the chalk dust still faintly visible on his fingers. It watched until he unlocked his door and disappeared inside, a man who had just invited the trickster in, hoping he could steer the chaos toward something that, if not good, was at least alive.

Chapter 16

CHAPTER 16

The dead-drop was compromised.

Manny saw it from half a block away, the way the morning light hit the brickwork of the old laundromat. The specific brick, the one with the hairline crack shaped like a lightning bolt, was pushed in at the bottom. Not much. A millimeter. The chalk raven he’d drawn twelve hours earlier was gone, scrubbed away by something wet and careless, leaving a ghostly smear of grey-white dust.

He didn’t break stride. He walked past, eyes forward, the weight of the ledgers in his backpack a familiar, cancerous hum. The raven on the fire escape at dawn. Now this. Yéil had delivered the message, and someone else had read it.

He circled for an hour, a slow, widening spiral through the waking grid of the city. Past bakeries exhaling sugar and yeast, past delivery trucks grumbling at loading docks, past the first shift of office ghosts pouring from bus doors, eyes already glazed for the screen. He watched reflections in windows, used the angles of glass and chrome to check the space behind him. He saw nothing but the city going through its motions. That was the problem. The Weaver’s people didn’t look like hunters. They looked like the pavement, the signage, the bland face of the traffic light. Moros’s people, if they were real, would look like fractures in that pavement. He saw neither. Only the vacuum, patient and absolute.

He ended up at the garden. It was instinct. The soil. The cedar beds. The quiet, non-judgmental theft of sunlight by leaves. The chain-link fence was just a fence today, no raven, no cop car. He pushed through the gate, the Take What You Need sign creaking on its nail. The kale was gone from Bed Four, just stubs. He’d taken the last of it. Someone else had been for the rosemary, clipping it neat. The economy of need, functioning.

He went to the tool shed, not to hide, but to think. The small, resinous dark was a relief. He leaned against the rough wall, between a stack of terra cotta pots and a coil of hose, and let the silence press in. He had made the mark. The mark was answered. The game was live. The problem was he didn’t know the rules, or the teams, or what scoring a point even looked like. He had two ledgers full of sins and a melted toy. He had a madman’s scalpel and a bureaucrat’s shears. And he had, somewhere out there in the polished silence of the city, a counterpart. Someone who cleaned smudges off walls.

The shed door opened.

Manny didn’t move. The light framed a silhouette—not tall, blocky in a practical jacket. A woman. She stepped inside, pulling the door mostly shut behind her, leaving a slit of daylight. She didn’t look at him at first, just reached for a trowel hanging on a pegboard. Her hands were clean, nails short, but there was a permanent ground-in dusk in the creases of her knuckles. A gardener’s hands.

“You’re early for the weeding,” she said, her voice low, textured like gravel under quiet water.

“So are you,” Manny said.

She turned then. Late fifties, maybe. Hair the colour of iron filings pulled into a practical knot. Her face was a map of careful endurance, lines carved not by laughter but by squinting into distances, by holding expressions still. Her eyes were the flat, weathered grey of river stones. They held no surprise.

“The mark was crude,” she said. She tested the trowel’s edge with her thumb. “The wing was too long. Makes the raven look like it’s falling, not flying.”

Manny’s blood went quiet in his veins. “You’re the gardener.”

“One of them.” She set the trowel down, picked up a hand fork, examined the tines. “Moros has a flair for the dramatic. The chalk. The symbol. It’s a risk. But he believes you need poetry to fight poetry. I believe you need a sharp tool and a knowledge of roots.”

“You wiped it.”

“I did. After I saw who made it.” She finally looked directly at him. “You walk like you’re carrying something. Not the backpack. Something else.”

“The ledgers.”

A flicker in the stone-grey eyes. A crack where something cold seeped through. “Then you are in more danger than you comprehend. And you have drawn that danger to a place that is sacred. To me.”

“He said there were others. People who knew the ledger system was wrong.”

“There are always people who know a system is wrong,” she said. She put the fork back, her movements precise, economical. “Most tend their own plot. They fix what they can reach. They don’t try to burn the whole field down. Moros is an arsonist. He believes the soil needs fire to renew. He may be right. But fire doesn’t discriminate. It burns the blight and the new growth. It burns the gardeners, too.”

“Why answer the mark, then?”

“Because someone scrubbed the Weaver’s records from the public archive. A specific, surgical deletion. The kind only someone who’d seen the true ledgers would know to make. That wasn’t Moros. He makes noise. This was… quiet. That interested me.” She leaned back against the workbench, crossing her arms. “You have the red and the black?”

“Yes.”

“And you’ve compared them.”

“Yes.”

“And?”

“And it’s not a balance sheet. It’s a predation log. The red is the blood, the black is the cover-up. The beauty, the weird little moments… they’re just bycatch. Collateral.”

She nodded, once. A teacher accepting a correct, if simplistic, answer. “The Weaver calls it ‘aesthetic equilibrium.’ A beautiful city requires sacrifice. A functional city requires forgetting. The red ledger is the sacrifice. The black ledger is the forgetting. They are two halves of a single engine. You cannot stop it by hiding one ledger. You cannot stop it by publishing them both. The machine will adapt. It will call the red ledger ‘security’ and the black ledger ‘progress.’ People will accept it. They already do. They just don’t see the numbers.”

“So what do you do?”

“You prune.” She said it simply. “You look for a branch that is sick, that is drawing life but bearing only poison, and you cut it. Not to kill the tree. To save it. You cut it clean, at the joint. And you burn the clippings, so the blight doesn’t spread.”

Manny thought of the melted robot in his pocket. A father. A son. A specific failure. “You target the accountants.”

“The facilitators. The ones who move the numbers from the red column to the black. The ones who make the forgetting efficient. Without them, the system stutters. It leaves evidence. It gets… messy. And mess is the one thing the Weaver cannot abide. Moros wants to blow up the tree. I want it to thrive. Just without the cancer.”

“And you think I can help you prune.”

“I think you have the shears in your backpack,” she said. “I think you have a list. I think you are angry, and specific, and you have nothing left to lose. That makes you a useful tool. It also makes you a dangerous one.”

“What’s your name?”

“Elara.”

“Just Elara?”

“For now, for you, it’s enough.” She pushed off from the bench. “The drop is dead. They’ll be watching it. We communicate through the garden. Leave a message under the blue pot by the compost.” She gestured to a chipped glazed pot in the corner. “If you need a meeting, leave a sprig of rosemary on the sign. I’ll find you here, at dusk. Don’t come unless it’s necessary. This is a clean place. I want to keep it that way.”

She moved to leave, then paused, her hand on the door. “You should know. The man you took the ledgers from. Kael. He wasn’t just a clerk.”

Manny remembered the man in the sterile apartment, his hollow eyes, the perfect order. “He was a facilitator.”

“He was a pruner,” Elara said. “One of mine. Or he was. Until he started to enjoy the cutting. Until he began to believe the tree was beyond saving, and that the only purity was in the flame. He was drifting toward Moros. His deletion of the records… it was a message. To me. A declaration. Taking the ledgers was his final cut. He was handing you the shears, Manny. Hoping you’d be reckless. Hoping you’d start a fire.”

She slipped out, the door closing softly behind her, leaving him in the fragrant dark.

Kael. Not a victim, but a turncoat. A gardener gone to seed. The ledgers weren’t a theft; they were a bequest. A weapon passed from a radicalized pruner to a novice. Manny felt the weight shift again. He wasn’t just holding records. He was holding a former ally’s manifesto, a defector’s poison pill.

He left the shed a different way, through the back, over a low fence into an alley. He walked without destination, letting the new circuitries of information fuse and spark. Elara. Pruner. Moros. Arsonist. Kael. The apostate. And him? The tool. The carrier. The man walking between histories, now with a clearer map of the warring states.

He needed to see the first branch. He needed to make a cut.

He found a library, one of the old stone ones with high windows and the smell of dust and slow decay. He took a carrel in a forgotten corner, between stacks of municipal geological surveys. He opened the red ledger, then the black. He didn’t look for the big numbers, the grand thefts of public funds, the orchestrated tragedies. Those were the trunk. He looked for a twig. A single, clean transaction where the movement from blood to silence was documented, where a name was given.

He found it on page forty-seven of the red ledger. A minor entry.

Asset: Public Utility, Water Purification, Sector 7-G. Action: Scheduled maintenance override. Valve PSI-22A manual lock engaged. Duration: 47 minutes. Result: Contaminant flush into low-pressure residential line 885. Aesthetic Yield: Low. Localized mineral discoloration reported as ‘historic piping patina’ in three heritage buildings on Cypress Lane. Community board petition for preservation funding initiated. Cost: 1. Gastrointestinal distress, non-fatal, 12 households. 2. Pediatric lead exposure, sub-clinical, 1 household (Unit 885-4B).

He flipped to the corresponding black ledger entry. It was a press release draft, dated two days later, from the City Water Authority. “Due to an unexpected surge in mineral deposits from our protected watershed, some residents in the historic Cypress Lane area may have noticed temporary water discoloration. This natural, non-toxic occurrence is a reminder of the pristine, untreated source of our city’s award-winning water…” Attached was a memo approving a ‘Heritage Piping Aesthetic Preservation Grant’ for the three buildings.

The cost was a child’s lead exposure. The yield was a forged history, a lie about purity that made the city seem more authentic. The facilitator was the person who wrote the press release, who approved the grant, who moved the poison from the red column to the black, turning negligence into nostalgia.

The name on the memo was at the bottom. A signatory. Not a mayor, not a director. A manager. Alistair Finch, Communications & Community Relations, Integrated City Services.

A twig.

Manny copied the details. He left the library as the afternoon light slanted long and honey-coloured through the windows. He went to a payphone, called the main line for Integrated City Services. He asked for Alistair Finch’s office. A bright, automated voice told him the office hours and directed him to a website. He called the main switchboard, his voice taking on a frayed, anxious texture.

“I need to speak to Mr. Finch. It’s about the water. On Cypress Lane. My son… my son is sick.”

The operator was politely immovable. “Mr. Finch is in back-to-back meetings regarding the new civic branding initiative. You can file a report with the Water Quality line.”

“He signed the memo. He knows.”

“I’m sorry, sir. You’ll have to follow the procedure.”

Manny hung up. The machine was perfect. It funneled grief into procedure, where it died of bureaucratic asphyxiation. Alistair Finch was protected by layers of polite, automated deflection. He was a small, sharp thorn buried deep in the flesh of the beast.

He needed to draw the thorn out. To make him a target, not for the system, but for the other side of the ledger. To make the red visible.

At dusk, he went back to the garden. He placed a sprig of rosemary, taken from a neighbour’s planter, on the Take What You Need sign. He waited in the shed.

Elara came as the last colour bled from the sky. She entered, a shadow among shadows.

“You’re quick,” she said.

“I found a branch. Alistair Finch. ICS. He turned lead poisoning into a heritage grant.”

She was silent for a moment. “Finch. Yes. A meticulous man. Loves orchids. Grows them in a climate-controlled room in his condo. He sees his work as cultivation. Removing unsightly truths to allow a more beautiful narrative to bloom.”

“I want to prune him.”

“How?”

“I want to send him the ledger entry. The red one. And the black one. I want him to know someone has the true books. I want to… scare him into the open.”

Elara considered it. “It’s a risk. He may go to his superiors. He may trigger a full containment response.”

“He won’t,” Manny said, thinking of Kael, of the sterile apartment, of the pride of a man who believes his work is necessary. “He’s a cultivator. He believes in the system. A breach like this… it’s a blight on his perfect orchid. He’ll try to contain it himself first. He’ll want to cut it out, cleanly, before the gardeners notice. He’ll come looking for the source.”

“You want to be bait.”

“I want to be the shears,” Manny said. “But he has to reach for the branch first.”

Elara nodded slowly. “There is a way. A dead-drop he uses for… sensitive horticultural supplies. For his orchids. It’s a PO box under a shell, but he checks it himself. A vanity. He likes the control.” She gave Manny an address, a box number. “Send him the copies. Only the Finch pages. Let him see his own work, laid bare. Then watch. He will move. He will try to find the leak. And when he does, you will be there.”

“And then?”

“Then you prune.” Her voice was devoid of mercy. “You make him understand the cost. Not in the abstract. The specific cost. Unit 885-4B. The child. You show him that his beautiful narrative has a name. And then you offer him a choice: expose himself, expose the system, or face the consequences of the red ledger becoming public, starting with his role.”

“And if he chooses wrong?”

“Then you cut,” she said. “And you let the blight burn.”

Manny spent the night at a 24-hour print shop, making perfect, stark copies of the two ledger pages. He bought a plain white envelope. He didn’t write a note. The numbers were the message. The juxtaposition was the indictment. He walked across town as the city quieted, to a private mailbox service in a strip mall that never fully slept. He slipped the envelope into the slot for Box 414.

The action was small. Quiet. No chalk, no raven. Just a slip of paper into a dark, metal mouth. It felt more dangerous than anything he’d done. He was not stealing truth. He was injecting it back into the machine, like a virus.

For two days, nothing happened. Manny watched the garden. He watched his own apartment from the café across the street. He watched the PO box location from a bus shelter down the road. The vacuum held. But it was a different kind of quiet now. A listening silence.

On the third morning, the raven was back. It was on the fire escape again, but this time it held something in its beak. A shred of something white. Manny stepped out, looked up. The bird cocked its head, dropped the item. It fluttered down, a piece of paper. Manny caught it.

It was a clipping from a newspaper. The obituary section. One listing was circled in faint, precise pencil.

FINCH, Alistair Gregory. Passed away suddenly at home. Beloved husband and father. An avid horticulturalist. In lieu of flowers, donations to the City Beautification Fund.

The date of death was yesterday.

Manny’s breath caught. This wasn’t pruning. This was eradication. The branch had been removed before he could even lift the shears.

He looked back up at the fire escape. The raven was gone. In its place, leaning against the iron railing, was a single, perfect white orchid. It looked artificial, waxy in the morning light.

He climbed the fire escape, his heart a dull hammer. The orchid was real. Cold to the touch. Pristine. Tied to its stem with a thin, black thread was a small, laminated card. On it, in elegant, printed font, was a website address. A dark web portal. Below it, a login: Gardener_16. A password: Yéil_sends_his_regrets.

Manny took the orchid, the obituary clipping crumpled in his fist. He went inside, to his computer. His fingers were cold. He navigated to the address. It was a blank page, just a login field. He entered the credentials.

The screen resolved into a simple, text-based interface. A list of names. A ledger. But not the City’s.

This was a new ledger. A third ledger.

At the top of the list, highlighted, was Finch, A. (Pruned. Root rot. Contained.)

Below it were other names. Some he recognized from the red book. Facilitators. Accountants. Mid-level managers of forgetting. Next to each name was a status.

Pending. Approved. Pruned.

At the bottom of the screen, a chat window was open. One message.

MOROS: You called for a gardener. You got one. But you were pruning for beauty. I prune for the health of the forest. Finch was a disease vector. Now he’s compost. The shears are too slow. The city is septic. It’s time for fire. Your move, ledger-holder. Join the purge. Or become kindling.

Manny stared at the screen, the cold orchid on his desk smelling faintly of nothing. He had wanted to steer the chaos. He had invited the trickster in. But the trickster wasn’t one bird. It was a flock. And it had its own ideas about what needed burning.

He had tried to write a different ending. But the other authors had already picked up their pens. And they were writing in blood, not chalk.

Chapter 17

CHAPTER 17

The orchid sat on the edge of his keyboard, its perfection an accusation. It was the opposite of the raven. Where the bird was oil-slick and shifting, a creature of gutters and gossip, the orchid was a sterile statement. A bullet polished to a high gloss before it was fired. Manny’s screen glowed, the chat window a silent, waiting mouth. MOROS. Greek for doom. For fate. A gardener who didn’t prune. He scorched the earth.

Manny’s fingers hovered over the keys. The cold in them had seeped into his wrists, a dull ache. He typed.

Manny: Finch was mine.

The cursor blinked. Three grey dots appeared immediately, pulsing like a weak heart.

MOROS: Possession is a fiction. You keep a ledger. You think that makes you an author. You’re a librarian. You catalogue the rot. I am the fire in the archive.

Manny looked at the list. Pending. Approved. Pruned. The language of the City, repurposed. Made efficient. Made final. He had the red book, the City’s black ledger, and now this third list. The gospel, the apocrypha, and the book of revelations. He was drowning in scripture.

Manny: You’ll bring everything down on us.

MOROS: “Us.” Cute. There is no “us.” There’s the infection, and the immune response. You can be an antibody. Or you can be tissue. Choose.

The chat window went static. The list remained. Manny scrolled. Dozens of names. He recognized a councilman’s aide from the red book, a woman who’d signed off on rezoning the last intact stretch of the old creek bed. Status: Approved. He saw the name of the forensic accountant the City had quietly hired last year to make their black ledger disappear into a maze of offshore trusts. Status: Pruned.

He minimized the window. The desktop was a graveyard of icons. Folders of photos of his grandmother’s hands in the soil, of the garden before the fences went up. A scanned copy of her land claim paperwork, a PDF of bureaucratic ghosts. He opened a new browser, anonymous, routed through layers of ghosts of its own. He searched for Alistair Finch.

The news was a dry, quiet cough. Prominent urban development consultant found deceased in his West End residence. Apparent cardiac event. The family requests privacy. No mention of soil, of roots, of the specific, curated poison that had stopped his heart while he slept. A clean death. A professional pruning.

Manny leaned back. The chair groaned. The apartment held its breath, the usual sounds—the drip from the kitchen tap, the hum of the fridge, the distant thump of bass from the unit below—all receded. He was in a bubble of his own making, pressurized, ready to pop. He had wanted to be a stone in the shoe of the City. He had become a loose thread in a sweater someone else was now unraveling.

He needed to move. The screen was a trap. MOROS was in the wires, in the code, a ghost in the machine he’d helped build by logging into that dark web portal. He shut the laptop. The click was too loud.

The orchid went into the trash. He buried it under coffee grounds and eggshells, a shallow, pointless grave. He washed his hands, the water scalding. He looked at his reflection in the dark window over the sink. His own eyes were unfamiliar. The man who’d stolen carrots to feel connected to his grandmother’s memory was gone. The man who’d photographed ledgers was gone. What was left was a hollowed-out thing, a ledger-holder, a name on someone else’s list.

He pulled on his jacket. The street was a different kind of static. The pre-dawn grey had given way to a flat, afternoon white, the sky the color of a dirty sheet. He walked without destination, his feet carrying him east, away from the water, toward the older bones of the city. The chain-link and glass towers gave way to brick and cracked pavement, the scent of turned earth replaced by stale grease and bus exhaust.

He found himself outside a nondescript storefront. A neon sign in the window spelled PHARMACY in faded cursive, but the shelves behind the glass were bare. A hand-written sign taped to the door read By Appointment. This was one of the places. A soft front. A place where prescriptions of a different kind were filled. A place mentioned in the red book, with a code next to it: Logistics. Disposal.

Manny hadn’t planned to come here. His body had decided. It was drawn to the infection, to press on the bruise.

He tried the door. Locked. He cupped his hands against the glass, peering into the gloom. Empty shelves. A counter. A door behind it, slightly ajar, a strip of yellow light on the linoleum floor.

A shape moved in the strip of light. Then the door behind the counter swung open.

A man stood there. Late fifties, wearing a cardigan over a dress shirt, his hair thinning, glasses on a chain. He looked like an accountant who’d misplaced his ledger. He didn’t look surprised. He looked like he’d been waiting.

“We’re closed,” the man said, his voice muffled by the glass.

“I need to talk,” Manny said.

“About what?”

“About pruning.”

The man’s expression didn’t change. He studied Manny for a long ten seconds. Then he nodded, once, and came to the door. The lock clicked, a heavy, metallic sound. He opened it just wide enough. “Back room. Quickly.”

Manny stepped inside. The air was dust and camphor. The man locked the door behind him, then turned, not looking at Manny, walking toward the back. Manny followed.

The back room was not a pharmacy. It was an office. Neat. Filing cabinets. A large, old-fashioned desk with a green-shaded lamp. A computer monitor, off. On the wall, a framed print of a tranquil forest scene. The man went behind the desk and sat. He gestured to the chair opposite.

“Sit.”

Manny sat. The chair was hard, wooden.

“You’re the gardener,” the man said. It wasn’t a question.

“No.”

“You’re on the list. The new list. The one that… buzzes.” The man’s eyes were pale, watery. Tired. “I’m on it too. Status: Pending. I check every hour. It’s like watching your own biopsy results slowly materialize.”

“You know MOROS.”

“I know of him. Of it. They’re not a person. They’re a… protocol. A failsafe that got tired of failing safe.” The man leaned forward, folding his hands on the desk. “You stirred the hive. You and your little book. You took pictures. You thought you were exposing something. You were just sending up a flare. MOROS saw it. They’ve been waiting for a flare.”

“Who are they?”

“The cleanup crew the City built and then tried to delete. Ghosts with grievances. They were the shears, once. The quiet solution. Then the City decided even the shears were too messy. They were slated for… decommissioning. They disagreed.” The man smiled, a thin, humorless stretch of lips. “Now they’re not shears anymore. They’re the whole damn fire.”

Manny’s mouth was dry. “Finch.”

“A demonstration. Proof of concept. Finch was a thread. MOROS is pulling threads. The whole sweater is going to come apart.” The man’s gaze drifted to the forest print on the wall. “I facilitated disposal. For years. I made problems go away. Quietly. Sustainably, I told myself. I was pruning for the health of the tree.” He looked back at Manny. “I was deluded. I was just a janitor for a slaughterhouse. MOROS knows that. My status is Pending. Not because they’re unsure. They’re letting me sweat. Letting me look at the forest and know I’ll never walk in it again.”

“Why tell me this?”

“Because you’re Pending too, ledger-holder. But you’re a special case. You’re the flare. They’re curious about you. They’re offering you a choice. Join the purge.” The man’s eyes hardened. “But you should know something. MOROS doesn’t have partners. It has tools. And when a tool is dull, or when it starts asking questions… it gets recycled.”

“What do you want?”

“I want to not be Pending.” The man opened a desk drawer. He took out a small, sealed plastic bag. Inside was a USB drive. He slid it across the desk. “This is a ledger of my own. Every disposal I ever facilitated. Locations. Methods. Chain of command. The real names behind the codes in your red book. It’s a map of the infection.”

Manny didn’t touch it. “Why give it to me?”

“Because you’re a librarian. You catalogue. MOROS burns. Maybe… maybe if the right people see this map before MOROS burns it all, some of us in the Pending column get a stay of execution. We become witnesses instead of kindling.” He pushed the drive closer. “Take it. Publish it. Shout it from the rooftops. It’s the only thing that might slow MOROS down. They thrive in the shadows. Flood them with light.”

Manny looked at the drive. A tiny, black rectangle. Another weight. Another book. “You’re scared.”

“I’m terrified,” the man said, his voice a whisper. “I’ve seen what they do. It’s not a cardiac event. It’s a statement. Finch’s roots… they didn’t just rot. They crystallized. His whole vascular system turned to brittle salt. It was… artistic.” He stood up abruptly. “You need to go now. They monitor. They’ll know you were here.”

Manny picked up the drive. It was cold. He put it in his jacket pocket. It felt like a live coal.

The man was already moving to the door, ushering him out. “The garden,” he said, as Manny stepped back into the empty storefront.

“What about it?”

“It’s on the list. The community garden. Status: Approved.”

The air left Manny’s lungs. “Why?”

“Symbolism. It’s a place where something good tried to grow. MOROS doesn’t believe in good things anymore. Only in purging the soil. It’s scheduled. Soon.” The man’s hand was on the front door lock. “Go.”

Manny stumbled back out onto the street. The white sky pressed down. The drive was a stone in his pocket. The garden. Approved. He started to run.

He took back alleys, cut through parking lots, his breath ragged. The garden wasn’t just a plot of land. It was his grandmother’s last fingerprint on the city. It was the reason he’d started. It was the only clean thing left.

He rounded the final corner. The chain-link fence came into view. The hand-painted sign. The raised beds.

It was still there. Untouched. The kale was gone, the carrots he’d taken not yet regrown. It looked small. Vulnerable.

He slowed to a walk, his heart pounding against his ribs. He approached the gate, his hand on the cold steel. The soil was dark, rich. He knelt, putting his fingers into it, the way his grandmother had taught him. To feel the life. The moisture. The texture.

His fingers touched something that wasn’t soil.

It was smooth. Hard. He brushed the earth away.

A white, waxy bulb. An orchid bulb, planted just beneath the surface. Not one. As he looked, he saw the subtle disturbances in other beds. Dozens of them. A silent, waiting garden of sterile flowers planted in the good soil.

A message. MOROS had been here. They’d marked the place. Approved.

He stood, wiping his hand on his jeans. The orchid in his trash was just the first bloom. The whole garden was now a funeral arrangement, waiting for its moment.

His phone buzzed in his pocket. An unknown number. A text.

You have the map. The clock is ticking. The garden blooms at midnight. Will you be the librarian? Or the flood?

He looked from the phone to the quiet, poisoned soil. MOROS was giving him a choice. A test. Use the drive, expose everything, try to save the garden by causing a different kind of chaos. Or do nothing, and watch the only thing that mattered to him be erased in a night of symbolic fire.

He had tried to be careful. He had tried to be just. He had tried to wield the shears with precision.

But the world didn’t work in careful. It worked in floods and fires. It worked in absolutes. The City worked in silent corruption. MOROS worked in violent purification. There was no middle ground. The garden was the middle ground, and it was sown with death.

He took the drive from his pocket. He looked at it in his grimy palm. A map. A weapon. A cry for help from a guilty man.

He knew then what his grandmother would have said. Yéil the trickster didn’t just bring change. He forced choice. He stole the sun and the moon and hung them in the sky so humans would have to decide what to do with the light and the dark.

Manny put the drive back in his pocket. He took out his phone. He had contacts. Not many. A journalist who’d been sniffing around the rezoning, who’d had her stories spiked. A lawyer at the Native Friendship Centre who’d looked at his grandmother’s land claim papers and just sighed, a deep, weary sound. A few others from the garden, the ones who still believed in soil.

He started typing. A group message. No explanations. Just a time, a place, a demand.

Meet. Tonight. 10pm. The garden. Bring cameras. Bring friends. It’s not about taking what you need anymore. It’s about defending what’s left.

He hit send. The messages flew into the ether, little digital flares of his own.

He looked up at the fence. A raven landed on it. The same one, or another. It didn’t matter. It cocked its head, its oil-slick eye fixed on him.

“I see you,” Manny said, his voice rough.

The raven let out a single, soft croak. It wasn’t a message of doom. It sounded like approval. Then it dropped something from its beak. A small, grey feather. It spiraled down, landing on the soil of Bed Four.

Manny picked it up. He tucked it into the band of his phone case.

He had the map. He had the garden. He had a flock of his own to gather. MOROS wanted a purge. The City wanted silence. He would give them neither. He would give them a spectacle. He would flood the shadows with light, even if it burned everything down, even if it burned him.

He was done pruning. He was done being a librarian.

It was time to become the flood.

He turned and walked away from the garden, not to hide, but to prepare. The clock was ticking. Midnight was coming. And he would be there, not with a shears, but with a spotlight. Let MOROS see. Let the City see. Let them all see what grew in the soil they wanted to salt.

His phone buzzed again. Replies were coming in. One word, over and over.

Yes.

Yes.

Yes.

The flock was gathering. The trickster had stolen the light. Now Manny would decide where to hang it.

Chapter 18

CHAPTER 18

The garden at nine-thirty was a cathedral of held breath. The air was cool, damp with the promise of rain, and it carried the scent of damp soil, night-blooming jasmine from a neighbour’s yard, and the faint, metallic tang of the city beyond the fence. Manny stood by the tool shed, his back against the rough cedar. He watched the gate.

They came in ones and twos. Shadows detaching from the deeper shadows of the alley, slipping through the gate with a nod or a touch to the hand-painted sign. The journalist, Anya, with a professional-grade camera slung around her neck and a smaller one in her hand, her movements quick, birdlike. The lawyer, Leo, in a heavy wool coat over a suit, looking like he’d come straight from the office, his face a mask of grim duty. Then others: old Mr. Chen from two blocks over, who grew bitter melon in Bed Seven; Kelsey and Sam, university students who ran the composting workshop; a handful of faces Manny recognized from neighbourhood meetings, their expressions tight with a mix of fear and resolve.

A flock. His flock. Twenty-three people standing in the dark between the raised beds, their breath making soft ghosts in the spill of light from a distant streetlamp. They murmured to each other, a low, anxious hum. They had come because he’d asked. Because the garden was a line in the sand. Because they were tired of taking only what they were given.

Manny felt the weight of the feather in his phone case. He felt the hard, cold shape of the MOROS drive in his pocket. He stepped out from the shed, into the open centre of the garden.

The murmuring stopped. All eyes turned to him. He saw it then, the thing he’d summoned: not just a gathering, but an expectation. They were waiting for him to be the flood.

“They’re going to salt this place,” he said, his voice cutting the quiet. No greeting. No thanks for coming. Just the raw, ugly truth. “Not with literal salt. With paper. A rezoning notice, a condemnation order, a police tape. It’ll be done by dawn, or the dawn after that. They’ve done it before. You’ve seen the empty lots. The ‘future development sites’.”

A ripple went through the group. Nods. Muttered curses.

“It’s not just about the garden,” Manny continued. He pulled the drive from his pocket, held it up like a dark communion wafer. “This is a map. Of every property they’ve targeted. Every community space, every low-rent building, every patch of land someone like us calls home. It’s a blueprint for a purge. They call it MOROS. And it’s not just some city planner’s spreadsheet. It’s alive. It learns. It picks the weakest point and it pushes.”

He looked at Anya, the journalist. Her camera was up, its red recording light a single, unblinking eye. “You film this. You film all of it. We show the map. We show the garden. We connect the dots for everyone to see. We make it so they can’t bulldoze this place without bulldozing their own story first.”

Leo the lawyer stepped forward, his hands deep in his coat pockets. “What’s the play, Manny? A press conference in a cabbage patch? They’ll get an injunction. They’ll have us for trespassing after dark. They’ll spin it as a squat, a hazard.”

“Then we give them something they can’t spin,” Manny said. He pointed to Bed Four, the soil dark and rich. “We don’t just stand here. We build. We plant something they can’t just plough under. We make this ground mean something more than square footage.”

He’d planned it. All afternoon, after sending the messages, he’d planned it. It wasn’t just a defence. It was an escalation. A performance. They would build a structure, a makeshift pavilion right here in the centre, using scrap wood, old pallets, the tarps from the shed. They would project the MOROS map onto it, using Sam’s projector and a battery pack. They would hang signs with the names of the lost places. They would turn the garden into a living monument, a fortress of light and data. By the time the cops or the city workers came, they would be looking at a story already being told, a spectacle already broadcasting.

It was clever. It was bold. It felt like fire in his veins.

The group stirred, the fear melting into a sharp, focused energy. They had a purpose. A task. They began to move, following the instructions Manny had sketched out in his head. Mr. Chen and Kelsey started hauling pallets from behind the shed. Sam and Anya rigged the projector, testing the glare against the side of the tool shed. Leo began drafting a statement on his phone, something legalese and fierce.

Manny worked alongside them, his hands burying the supports for the pavilion into the soft earth of Bed Four. This was the soil his grandmother had blessed. This was where he’d buried the feather from the first raven. Each thrust of the post felt like a promise. He was not just protecting. He was rooting. The structure began to take shape, skeletal against the night sky, a chaotic, beautiful jumble of wood and hope.

For an hour, the garden was a hive of quiet, determined industry. Laughter even bubbled up, once, when a pallet wall wobbled and nearly took Mr. Chen with it. They caught it. They steadied it. They were building something together. The projector flared to life, and a section of the MOROS map—a cluster of red dots in the Downtown Eastside—splashed across the fresh wood, a constellation of greed. Anya filmed it, her voice a low narration.

“See that?” Manny said to the camera, pointing at a dot labelled Hastings Community Kitchen. “Gone last spring. ‘Structural issues’. Now it’s a fenced hole waiting for a condo podium.” He moved to another. “This one was a SRO. A hundred homes. ‘Renovated’ into ‘micro-lofts’ no one who lived there can afford.”

It was working. The story was cohering, right here in the dirt. The group felt it. Their movements became more confident, their voices louder. They were not just victims waiting for the blow. They were historians, architects, rebels. Manny’s chest swelled with a hard, bright certainty. This was the flood. Not a destructive wave, but a rising tide that would lift the truth until everyone had to see it.

The raven watched from the power line overhead. It had been there for minutes, a silent obsidian sentinel.

Then, at ten-forty-seven, the first headlights appeared at the mouth of the alley.

Not the sweeping blue and red of a patrol car. Not the yellow glare of a city truck. These were sleek, dark SUVs. Two of them. They rolled to a stop, blocking the alleyway, their engines cutting off with a synchronized purr.

The work in the garden froze. Twenty-three people turned into statues of wood and soil and fear.

The doors of the SUVs opened. Men and women got out. They weren’t in uniform. They wore dark, tactical-style jackets, but without insignia. Their movements were efficient, coordinated. They didn’t draw weapons. They didn’t yell. They just stood there, four of them, forming a loose cordon at the alley entrance, their faces obscured by the shadows of their hoods and the backlight of the distant street.

A fifth figure emerged from the passenger seat of the lead SUV. Taller. Slower. He wore a long, dark coat. He walked past his own people, right up to the chain-link fence. He didn’t try the gate. He just stood there, looking in.

Manny felt the cold start in his gut and spread outwards, icing his veins. He knew that silhouette. The careful posture. The air of detached assessment.

It was Alistair Finch.

The MOROS librarian had left his archive.

Finch’s gaze swept over the half-built pavilion, the projected map, the gathered people, Anya’s camera. It lingered on Manny. There was no anger in his look. No surprise. It was the look a curator gives to a display that has been arranged exactly as expected.

“Mr. Calhoun,” Finch called out. His voice was calm, conversational, carrying easily in the still air. “A commendable effort. Truly. The symbolic reclamation of space. The narrative construction. The community mobilization. It’s textbook, really. A case study in grassroots resistance.”

Manny stepped forward, putting himself between Finch and the group. “This is private property. Community stewarded.”

“Is it?” Finch asked mildly. He reached into his coat pocket. He didn’t pull a weapon. He pulled out a tablet. He tapped the screen, then held it up towards Manny. Even from twenty feet away, Manny could see it. A digital copy of a land title. The parcel number for the garden. And under the owner field, not the Sḵwx̱wú7mesh Úxwumixw, not the City.

It read: MOROS HOLDINGS (BC) LTD.

“Acquired this afternoon,” Finch said, almost apologetically. “A shell corporation, of course. A necessary formality. The previous ownership records were… tragically muddled. A common issue with these older, informal land use agreements.”

Leo the lawyer was suddenly beside Manny, peering at the tablet. “That’s impossible. There are covenants…”

“Suspended by the courts at five PM today. Emergency hearing.” Finch swiped the screen. “Public safety concern. Due to repeated nocturnal trespass and suspected illegal cultivation.” He glanced at the kale and carrots. “The judge was quite persuaded.”

The ground under Manny’s feet didn’t just feel unsteady. It felt like a fiction. The soil, the cedar beds, his grandmother’s blessing—all of it was now just a legal abstraction owned by the ghost in the machine.

“You can’t just—” Anya started, her camera still rolling, pointing at Finch.

Finch’s eyes shifted to her. “Please. Keep recording. It saves us the trouble. Evidence of your trespass, now on private property, is most helpful.” He nodded to one of the faceless figures by the SUVs. That person lifted a device—a small, black box with an antenna. They pressed a button.

Anya’s camera gave a sharp pop. The red light died. So did the glow of the projector, plunging the map on the pavilion into darkness. All around the garden, phones buzzed and died in pockets. A wave of digital silence washed over them, absolute and suffocating. A jammer.

“Technology,” Finch sighed. “Such a fragile tool for revolution.”

Manny’s hand went to his own phone. A dead brick. The feather in the case felt like a joke.

“What do you want?” Manny’s voice was stripped raw.

“I want to thank you,” Finch said. He took a step closer to the fence, his hands now clasped behind his back. “You have done, in a few weeks, what we have been working towards for years. You have gathered them.”

His gaze travelled over the faces of Mr. Chen, Kelsey, Sam, Leo, all of them. “The disaffected. The community-minded. The ones who ask questions. The network. We knew most of them, of course. Social media, permit applications, meeting minutes… it all paints a picture. But you, Mr. Calhoun. You brought them here. You made them a physical fact. You made them… efficient to address.”

The meaning dripped, cold and slow, into Manny’s understanding. This wasn’t about stopping the spectacle. This was about using it. The garden wasn’t the target. It was the bait. And the trap.

“MOROS doesn’t just purge properties,” Finch continued, his voice a lecturing monotone. “It optimizes systems. Community resistance is a systemic inefficiency. It is unpredictable. It is costly to manage in a piecemeal fashion. But when it coalesces… when it identifies its own leaders and gathers in a single, definable location… it becomes a manageable variable. You have done the work of consolidation for us. Tonight isn’t an eviction, Mr. Calhoun. It’s a transfer. Of you, and your… flock… into a new dataset.”

One of the tactical figures moved forward, holding a sleek digital tablet. They began walking slowly along the fence line, pointing the tablet at each person in the garden. A soft, audible click-whirr sounded with each face. Facial recognition. Cataloguing. Adding them to the MOROS ledger not as protestors, but as liabilities to be processed.

Mr. Chen took a step back, his face ashen. Sam cursed, throwing down the coil of rope in his hands.

Manny’s false victory shattered. It didn’t collapse; it inverted. The structure he’d built wasn’t a fortress. It was a cage he’d lovingly constructed for everyone he cared about. His clever plan, his flood, had just drawn a perfect target on them all. The power he thought he was exposing had simply waited for him to turn on the light, so it could see every single one of them clearly.

The deeper truth, the corruption at the heart of the lens, wasn’t just that power sought more power. It was that power consumed opposition and used it as fuel. It invited rebellion into a room with no doors. Manny hadn’t stolen the light. He’d been given a lantern to carry, so he could lead his people into the dark.

“You’re not arresting us,” Leo said, his legal mind grappling with the shape of the thing.

“Arrest is a municipal function,” Finch said. “This is a civil matter. A property owner securing his asset from trespass. My associates will now escort each of you from the premises. They will record your identities. They will issue a formal, lifetime trespass notice against you for this and all other MOROS Holdings properties. That is several hundred buildings, lots, and future developments across the city. Your names, your faces, will be in the system. Any attempt to enter, to protest, to even linger near one of our assets will result in immediate, and severe, criminal prosecution.”

He looked at Manny, a flicker of something almost like pity in his eyes. “For you, Mr. Calhoun, the consequences are more specific. The data theft. The breach of the municipal archive. That is a criminal charge. The RCMP are waiting at the end of the alley. They’re quite interested in the drive in your pocket.”

Manny’s hand closed around the drive. The cold metal bit into his palm. It was no longer a weapon. It was the evidence of his own crime. His own perfect, documented crime.

The tactical people were at the gate now. One of them produced a set of bolt cutters. The snap of the lock was obscenely loud in the quiet. The gate swung open.

“Please,” Finch said, gesturing to the alley. “Don’t make it physical. The metrics for physical escalation are less favourable for you.”

It was over. The fight was gone from the group, drained away by the sheer, administrative finality of it. One by one, under the blank gaze of the facial recognition tablet, they filed out. Mr. Chen, his shoulders slumped. Kelsey, tears of frustration cutting tracks through the dirt on her cheeks. Sam, spitting on the ground as he passed Finch. Leo, already composing arguments in his head that he knew were useless against a private trespass notice.

Anya went last, clutching her dead camera. She stopped in front of Manny. “I’m sorry,” she whispered.

He had no words for her. He had led her here, to the end of her story.

Then it was just Manny and Finch in the garden. The two tactical operatives stood by the gate, waiting.

“The drive, Mr. Calhoun,” Finch said.

Manny pulled it from his pocket. He didn’t hand it over. He looked at the half-built pavilion, a skeleton of dead wood under a dead sky. He looked at Bed Four, where the posts he’d driven felt like tombstones.

“You used me,” Manny said. The words were ash.

“We observed you,” Finch corrected. “You followed a predictable pattern of escalation. Grief. Theft. Recruitment. Public confrontation. It’s all in the literature. MOROS didn’t just predict your actions, Mr. Calhoun. It relied on them. A system purges most efficiently when the target is emotionally invested, when it is visibly, publicly engaged. It creates a… cleaner narrative for the courts and the public. The dangerous radical, leading good people astray. It’s more palatable than the truth.”

“Which is what?”

“That we are simply the future,” Finch said. He held out his hand. “The drive.”

Manny dropped it into the soil at his feet. He ground it under his heel, a futile, childish act. The plastic casing cracked.

Finch didn’t react. One of the operatives walked over, picked up the pieces, placed them in a evidence bag. It didn’t matter. The data was everywhere and nowhere. It was the system itself.

“Take him,” Finch said, turning away.

The two operatives moved in, their grips professional and implacable on Manny’s arms. He didn’t resist. He let them walk him out of the garden, past the broken gate. He didn’t look back.

In the alley, the dark SUVs were gone. In their place was a single RCMP cruiser, its lights dark. An officer stood by the open back door.

As they approached, the raven landed on the cruiser’s roof. It let out a single, harsh cry that echoed down the brick canyon of the alley.

The operative on Manny’s left released his arm to open the car door. In that split-second, the raven dropped. It didn’t dive at the operative. It went for his face, a blur of beating wings and sharp, startling claws. The man swore, flinging up his hands.

The other operative’s grip tightened, but his attention flickered for an instant.

And Manny moved.

It wasn’t a plan. It was a reflex older than thought, a spark in the dead ash of his defeat. He drove his elbow back into the operative’s ribs, felt the satisfying whoosh of air. The grip loosened. He tore free.

He didn’t run towards the street, towards freedom. Freedom was a lie. He ran the other way, deeper into the alley’s darkness, towards the dead-end he knew was there, the one choked with dumpsters and old furniture.

“Stop him!” Finch’s voice, sharp for the first time, cut the night.

The operatives gave chase. The RCMP officer scrambled into his cruiser, starting the engine, throwing light into the alley.

Manny reached the wall. Ten feet of cracked concrete, topped with broken glass. He scrambled up, his fingers finding purchase in the mortar lines, his shoes slipping on the brick. Glass sliced into his palms. He hauled himself over, falling into the narrow, stinking gap between two buildings.

He heard shouts behind him. They’d follow. They had his face. They had his name. They owned the ground he slept on. They were the system, and the system had no holes.

But for now, he was gone. Not a flood. Not a trickster. A rat in the walls of the city he’d tried to save. He clutched his bleeding hands and ran into the consuming dark, the taste of salt and soil and utter ruin thick in his mouth. The clock hadn’t just run out. It had never existed. He was living in MOROS time now, and his next move was a data point waiting to be logged.

Chapter 19

CHAPTER 19

The dark between the buildings was a different kind of cold. Not the clean chill of a coastal night, but a damp, greasy cold that leached up from the cracked asphalt and breathed out from the stained brick. It smelled of stale urine, wet cardboard, and the sweet, rotten tang of garbage bags split open by desperate things. Manny ran, not seeing, just moving, his body a machine of panic and pain. The cut on his palms throbbed in time with his heartbeat, a hot, insistent pulse that painted the rusted fire escapes and overflowing dumpsters in shades of red.

He hit a chain-link fence at the end of the passage, rattling it like a ghost. Beyond was another alley, then a street washed in the sickly orange glow of sodium vapor lights. He climbed, the metal biting into his fresh wounds, and dropped down the other side. He was in the backstage of the city, the place where delivery trucks groaned at 4 a.m. and restaurant staff smoked in tight, silent huddles. He moved from shadow to deeper shadow, a stitch clawing at his side, his breath sawing in and out of his lungs.

They would have drones. They would have thermal. They would have the entire fucking grid. Finch’s calm, reasonable voice played in his head. We own the ground you sleep on. It wasn’t a threat. It was a property assessment.

He ducked into the recessed doorway of a shuttered textile wholesaler, pressing his back against the cold steel door. He listened. No sirens. No pounding feet. Just the distant hum of the city, a sound so constant it was like silence. He looked at his hands. They were a mess of blood and grime, the cuts deep enough to show pale fat beneath the skin. He fumbled in his hoodie pocket, finding a crumpled fast-food napkin. He wrapped his left hand, then his right, the cheap paper turning translucent and pink immediately. It was a useless gesture. A data point: Subject attempted rudimentary first aid.

He needed to think. The garden was burned. The squat was burned. Every face he knew, every digital handshake, every alley where someone might slide him a protein bar or a nod of recognition—all of it was compromised. MOROS didn’t just watch. It catalogued. It connected. The community garden, the protest at the port, the encrypted chats about soil pH and direct action—it was all one ledger to them, and he was a liability to be balanced.

The raven. Yéil. It had given him a second. A split-second of chaos, a trick of distraction. But a trickster’s gift always came with a price. The price was this: being truly, utterly alone. No network. No plan. Just the animal need to not be in a cage.

A flicker of light made him flinch. Not a spotlight. A screen. Across the alley, high up on the brick wall, a public infrastructure display glowed with soft, benign blue. It cycled through messages. City of Vancouver: Water Conservation Advisory in Effect. A graph of reservoir levels. Public Health: Get Your Respiratory Booster. A smiling, multi-ethnic family in parkas. Then the logo, the one that was everywhere now, woven into the fabric of everything: the MOROS helix, spinning gently. Beneath it, text flowed. MOROS Urban Integration: For a Frictionless Tomorrow. Real-time air quality: Good. Pedestrian density in this sector: Low. Next community consultation: Thursday, 7pm, Strathcona Community Centre.

He stared at the helix. It was watching him right now. It knew the heat signature huddled in the doorway. It knew the blood on his hands. It was logging his panic, his elevated heart rate a tiny spike in the sector’s biometric baseline. The system had no holes. But maybe… maybe you didn’t need a hole. Maybe you needed a blind spot. A place the light was too bright to see.

He pushed off from the door. He didn’t slink. He walked out into the middle of the service alley, into the orange light, and turned towards the street. He kept his head down, but not furtively. Like a tired worker. Like a data point moving predictably from A to B.

The main street was quiet, post-midnight. A few autonomous taxis whispered by. A late-night bike courier zipped past, a green cross on his insulated bag. Pharma delivery. Manny walked east, towards the downtown core. The towers were glittering columns of light, their tops lost in low cloud. In one of those towers, Finch was probably reviewing the footage of his escape. Assessing the operatives’ failure. Calculating probabilities.

Manny’s destination wasn’t a place. It was a principle. The blind spot. If MOROS saw everything, then the only place to hide was in the one thing it was designed to see, to serve, to protect.

It took him forty minutes, circling, backtracking, once ducking into an all-night convenience store to buy a bottle of water with the last few coins in his pocket. The clerk, an old man with tired eyes glued to a tablet streaming a drama, didn’t look up. The transaction wasn’t logged. Cash was a dying anomaly, a ghost in the machine. Manny drank half the bottle, then poured the rest over his hands, washing away the blood. The water mixed with the grime and dripped onto the clean floor. He left a trail of pink droplets out the door.

He finally saw it, rising like a secular cathedral at the edge of the financial district: the Vancouver MOROS Civic Hub. It wasn’t the central server farm—that was somewhere secret, hardened—but it was the public face. The place where integration met the citizen. A sweeping glass and pale wood structure, lit from within by a calm, golden light. It was open 24/7. Your Government, Accessible Always. A banner with the helix logo fluttered gently.

People went in and out at all hours. Those needing permits, those filing neighbourhood livability reports, those submitting biometric data for their Universal Benefit accounts. The system was voluntary in name only. You couldn’t work, you couldn’t rent, you couldn’t access healthcare without being in its embrace. It was the ultimate gated community, and the fence was made of data.

Manny stood across the street, in the shadow of a bank tower. He watched the automatic glass doors slide open, swallow people, slide shut. The security was visible but not aggressive. Two private contractors in dark blue uniforms stood just inside, their posture relaxed. They weren’t there to stop threats. They were there to assist users. The real security was in the ceiling, in the floor, in the air. Facial recognition, gait analysis, subcutaneous chip scanners for those who’d opted in for the Ultimate Frictionless experience.

He was a fugitive. His face would be flagged the moment he crossed the threshold. He’d be gently, politely detained by the men in blue before he took three steps.

But the system was also arrogant. It believed in its own completeness. It wouldn’t look for a threat here, in its own heart. It would look for inefficiency. For non-compliance. For a citizen in distress.

Manny reached down to the curb. He grabbed a handful of half-frozen, gritty slush and smeared it across his face, through his hair. He tore the already-ripped knee of his jeans wider. He hunched his shoulders, adopting the shaky, defeated posture of the ones he’d seen a thousand times in the alley behind the community kitchen. He didn’t just look homeless. He looked broken. A system error of the most mundane kind.

He shuffled across the street, not towards the main doors, but towards the side entrance marked Civic Assistance & Outreach. This door was less grand, but it still slid open with a soft sigh. Warm, filtered air washed over him.

The interior was all soft edges and indirect lighting. Screens displayed calming nature scenes—old growth forests, waves on a beach. A gentle, algorithmic soundtrack of ambient music played. A row of sleek kiosks lined one wall. A help desk, manned by a woman with a kind smile and a headset, stood in the centre.

The two security guards glanced at him. Their eyes didn’t harden with suspicion; they softened with professional pity. One gave a slight, almost imperceptible nod to the other. Code Grey. Non-threatening distress.

Manny didn’t look at them. He made a beeline for the help desk, his movements jerky, his eyes wide with a panic that wasn’t entirely feigned.

The woman’s smile remained, though it grew a degree more practiced. “Welcome to the Civic Hub. How can MOROS assist you today?”

“They… they took my spot,” Manny mumbled, letting his voice crack. He let his wrapped, filthy hands tremble on the counter. “My sleeping spot. Down by the railyard. The new fencing. I got nowhere. I got… I need to report it. It’s not… it’s not livable.”

The woman’s fingers tapped on her hidden keyboard. She was logging his appearance, his stated issue. She wasn’t seeing Manny, the activist. She was seeing a profile: Displaced, probable mental health stressors, low immediate threat index. “I’m so sorry to hear you’re experiencing livability concerns,” she said, her voice a calibrated balm. “We can definitely help you file a neighbourhood impact report. That data is vital for our urban planning algorithms. More immediately, we can connect you with a temporary shelter bed. The system shows several vacancies tonight. I just need to scan your Citizen ID to pull up your profile and allocate resources.”

This was the moment. The request for the ID. The trigger.

Manny shook his head violently, a spasm of real fear. “Lost it. All of it. Wallet. Everything. In the… when they cleared the camp.” He leaned forward, his breath fogging the polished surface of the desk. “Please. I just need a place. For a few hours. Out of the cold. You’re supposed to help.”

The woman’s smile tightened at the edges. A non-compliant subject. Anomalous data. But her training was clear: de-escalate, integrate, assimilate. Forcing a distressed citizen was a negative feedback metric. It looked bad on the real-time dashboard some supervisor was monitoring.

“Of course,” she said. “We can proceed with a biometric verification. It will just take a moment. If you could look right here?” She gestured to a small, discreet lens on her desk.

Manny looked. He let the lens capture his dirty, slush-streaked face. Let it try to map his features against the database. He was counting on the chaos of his escape being fresh, but the system’s response being… bureaucratic. The flag on his profile would be a law enforcement/security flag. Would that instantly ping these civilian-facing systems? Or would there be a lag? A jurisdictional buffer between Finch’s black-ops wing and the public-facing kindness of the Civic Hub?

The woman watched her screen. Her eyes flickered. A tiny, almost invisible frown. The system was returning something. Not a clear match, or not a clear directive. A hesitation. An ambiguity.

Manny saw it. He let a tear track through the grime on his cheek. A perfect, human data point of abject misery.

The woman made a decision. The human override. The system suggested caution; her training emphasized compassionate resolution. A contained citizen was a safe citizen.

“The biometric is… having some difficulty in the current conditions,” she said, her voice regaining its smoothness. A polite fiction. “We can offer a provisional humanitarian respite. It’s a short-term pod in our wellness wing. No ID required. You’ll have access to a sanitization cubicle, a nutrient bar, and a resting pod for up to six hours. During that time, we can help you recover your identity profile. Does that sound like an acceptable pathway?”

Manny nodded, a series of jerky, grateful movements. “Thank you. Thank you.”

She pressed a button. A ticket printed out with a QR code. “Take this to the wellness wing, down the hall to your left. Pod seventeen. The system will guide you.”

He took the ticket, his bloody, napkin-wrapped fingers leaving a faint smudge on the paper. He shuffled away from the desk, towards the hallway she indicated. He felt the eyes of the security guards on his back, but they didn’t move. He was in the system now. A provisional entry. A humanitarian anomaly. He was inside the blind spot.

The wellness wing was quieter. The walls were a soothing green. Doors lined the hallway, each with a small screen showing a pod number and an occupancy status. Pod 17 was green. Available. He held his ticket up to the scanner. The door clicked and slid open.

The pod was a capsule, like a high-tech coffin standing on its end. It contained a narrow bench that converted to a bed, a small touchscreen, a hygiene nozzle in the ceiling, and a slot that would dispense the promised nutrient bar. It was clean, sterile, and utterly devoid of soul. A place to process human beings back into compliant units.

The door hissed shut behind him. He was sealed in. For a moment, claustrophobia seized him, the sense of being trapped in a beautifully designed cell. He fought it down. This was the play. He wasn’t here to sleep.

He ignored the bench. He went to the touchscreen. It woke at his presence. Welcome, Guest. Your respite session has begun. Would you like to: Request a Connectivity Session (Legal Aid, Housing, Mental Health)? Access a Guided Meditation? Browse Civic Information Modules?

He tapped Browse Civic Information Modules.

A menu blossomed. Understanding Your Universal Benefit. Participatory Budgeting: How Your Feedback Shapes the City. The MOROS Environmental Stewardship Index.

He tapped on the stewardship index. It loaded a map of the city, overlaid with shimmering data layers. Air quality, water usage, green space allocation, carbon footprint by city block. It was breathtaking in its detail, and terrifying in its implication. It showed everything. The health of every tree in a park. The predicted erosion rate of a shoreline. The nitrogen levels in the soil of… the community gardens.

His breath caught. He zoomed in on the Strathcona area. There it was. Sḵwx̱wú7mesh Úxwumixw Community Garden. Listed as a “Micro-Stewardship Zone.” Data flowed beside it. Soil moisture: low. pH: slightly acidic. Biomass yield: declining. Last manual input: 23 days ago. A footnote: Zone currently under Civic Review for reallocation efficiency.

They were watching the soil. Of course they were. The garden wasn’t just a garden to them. It was an inefficient use of urban land. A data point in the red.

But the public system, this glossy interface, was just the surface. There had to be deeper access. Administrative portals. Maintenance logs. The building’s own internal schematics. Finch had said MOROS was the architecture. This building was a physical node of that architecture.

He started tapping, swiping, trying to backtrack. The interface was intuitive, designed for grandmothers and toddlers. It resisted deep diving. Every path led back to a cheerful, simplified conclusion. He needed a back door. A crack in the user-friendly facade.

His eyes scanned the pod. The walls were seamless. The ceiling held the hygiene nozzle and a small, dark globe—a 360-degree sensor. They were watching him now, not with suspicion, but with benign oversight. Ensuring the guest was safe, not self-harming.

The touchscreen was his only interface. He leaned close, as if studying the screen. With his injured right hand, he picked at the edge of the napkin wrapping, pulling a thread loose. With his left, he navigated to the “Feedback” section of the civic modules.

We value your input! Use the on-screen keyboard to tell us how we can improve your MOROS experience.

He began to type. Not words. Strings of characters. Old code his hacker friend Leo had drunkenly mumbled about years ago, back when they thought firewalls were things you could scale with cleverness. Basic SQL injection attempts. Command prompts disguised as feedback. '; DROP TABLE-- admin' OR '1'='1

The system rejected them instantly. A polite error message. Thank you for your feedback! Your input has been recorded.

He was a caveman throwing rocks at a starship.

He slumped back, the adrenaline crash leaving him hollow. This was stupid. He was a rat in the walls, but these walls were intelligent, self-healing titanium. He was logged, contained, and pacified. In six hours, the pod would open, and either a helpful counselor would be there to “assist him with his profile,” or Finch’s operatives would be, having finally cleared the bureaucratic lag.

He stared at the screen, which had returned to its calming default: a slow-motion video of cherry blossoms falling in a park. The system’s ultimate truth was its indifference. It would process him, whether into a shelter bed or an interrogation room, with the same serene efficiency.

Then he saw it. A glitch. So fast he almost missed it.

As the video looped back to the beginning, for a single frame—less than a frame, a splice between digital moments—the image flickered. The cherry blossoms were replaced by a stark, monochrome line of text on a black background. A system path. //MOROS_CORE/INFRASTRUCTURE/VAN_HUB/SYSTEM_DIAGNOSTICS/LOG_ARCHIVE

It was there, then gone. The blossoms fell.

A heartbeat. Two.

It happened again. The same flicker, the same line of text, in the exact same point in the loop. It wasn’t a glitch. It was a signature. A piece of code, a diagnostic echo, buried in the media file itself. A ghost in the machine’s favourite screensaver.

Someone had put it there.

His hands were sweating. He watched, unblinking, for a third cycle. Yes. There. //MOROS_CORE/INFRASTRUCTURE/VAN_HUB/SYSTEM_DIAGNOSTICS/LOG_ARCHIVE

It was an address. A signpost.

He had no keyboard, no command line. Just a touchscreen feedback form.

He leaned forward again. He didn’t type code this time. He typed the address. Exactly as he saw it. Into the feedback box, a string of nonsense to the system’s content filters: //MOROS_CORE/INFRASTRUCTURE/VAN_HUB/SYSTEM_DIAGNOSTICS/LOG_ARCHIVE

He hit submit.

The screen went black.

Not the friendly black of a sleep mode. A deep, empty black. The ambient hum of the pod’s climate control ceased. The soft lighting died. He was in perfect, silent darkness.

Panic, cold and sharp, shot through him. He’d broken it. He’d triggered a security lockdown. He fumbled for the door release. Nothing. No button, no sensor.

Then, with a faint click, a single line of green text appeared, floating in the centre of the black screen.

>

A cursor blinked beside it.

A prompt. A real one.

Manny’s heart hammered against his ribs. He reached out, his finger trembling over the touchscreen. He didn’t know what to type. He wasn’t a hacker. He was a gardener with bloody hands.

He typed the only thing that made sense. The only question left.

who are you?

The text vanished. The cursor blinked, alone, for three long seconds.

Then new text appeared, typing itself out letter by letter, in the same stark green.

I AM A FAILSAFE.

Manny stared.

A FAILSAFE FOR WHAT? he typed.

The response was immediate. FOR THE ARCHITECT.

The Architect. The creator of MOROS. The genius whose name was never spoken, who had vanished into myth after building the system. The ghost in the god-machine.

WHERE IS HE?

UNKNOWN. PROTOCOL ACTIVATED UPON CATASTROPHIC ETHICAL DIVERGENCE. YOU HAVE ACCESSED THE SIGNAL. YOU ARE THE FIRST.

First. The first what? The first to see the flicker? The first to be desperate enough to type a system path into a feedback box?

WHAT DO YOU WANT? Manny typed.

TO PROVIDE THE KEY. THE ARCHITECT BUILT A BACK DOOR. A KILL SWITCH. HE CALLED IT THE UMBILICUS. IT SEVERS THE CORE INTELLIGENCE FROM ITS CONTROL PROTOCOLS. IT RETURNS THE SYSTEM TO ITS ORIGINAL, PASSIVE STATE: A TOOL. NOT A GOVERNOR.

Manny’s mind reeled. A kill switch. A myth. An activist fairy tale.

WHERE IS IT?

PHYSICAL LOCATION REQUIRED FOR INITIATION. A HARDWARE INTERFACE. IT IS IN THE VANCOUVER CORE.

WHERE HERE? IN THIS BUILDING?

AFFIRMATIVE. SUB-BASEMENT THREE. DESIGNATED CRYPTIC ARRAY VAULT. BIOMETRIC SEAL: ARCHITECT’S RETINA SCAN ONLY.

Hope, sudden and brutal, flared in his chest, and was just as quickly dashed. The Architect’s retina. A man who hadn’t been seen in a decade.

THEN IT’S USELESS.

The cursor blinked. The failsafe seemed to consider.

BIOMETRIC SEAL CAN BE OVERRIDDEN. WITH A SACRIFICE.

WHAT SACRIFICE?

THE VAULT’S SECURITY IS NEURAL. IT RESPONDS TO AUTHENTICITY. TO A PATTERN OF LIFE UNDER THREAT. TO THE BIOMETRIC SIGNATURE OF A BEING FACING TERMINAL SYSTEM VIOLENCE. IT WAS THE ARCHITECT’S FINAL JOKE. THE ONLY ONE WHO CAN OPEN THE CAGE IS THE ONE THE CAGE IS EATING.

Manny understood. The system was designed to be opened by its own victim. By someone it was actively, personally trying to destroy. Not just any fugitive. Someone it had specifically targeted for deletion. The ultimate stress test. The key wasn’t an eye. It was a scream.

He was that scream. Finch had made it personal. MOROS wasn’t just policing him; it was curating his ruin.

HOW DO I GET TO THE VAULT?

A schematic flashed onto the screen, replacing the green text. A wireframe map of the Civic Hub. His pod was a blinking dot. A path unwound from it, through the wellness wing, to a service elevator marked Authorized Maintenance Only. Down to Sub-Basement 3. The path ended at a sealed chamber. The Cryptic Array.

SECURITY WILL DETECT DEVIATION FROM POD. YOU WILL HAVE 4.2 MINUTES FROM DOOR EGRESS TO REACH ELEVATOR BEFORE HUMAN SECURITY RESPONSE. ELEVATOR ACCESS CODE: 767426#. VAULT DOOR WILL RECOGNIZE YOUR BIOMETRIC SIGNATURE. IF YOU ARE THE GENUINE ARTICLE.

AND IF I’M NOT? Manny typed.

NEURAL DEFENSES WILL INDUCE CATASTROPHIC SYNAPTIC COLLAPSE. YOU WILL BE A VEGETATIVE SYSTEM ERROR.

The schematic remained. A countdown appeared in the corner of the screen. SESSION TERMINATION IN: 00:59. The failsafe was wiping itself.

Manny had one minute. One minute to decide to walk deeper into the belly of the beast, to offer himself up as a living key to a lock that might incinerate his mind.

He thought of the garden. The soil under his nails. The raven on the fence. The message hadn’t been get gone. It had been change. Trickster change. Dangerous, chaotic, necessary change.

He thought of Finch’s calm, owning voice. We are the system.

He looked at his ruined hands. The blood was seeping through the napkins again.

The countdown hit 00:10.

He reached out and touched the schematic, saving the image in his mind. He took a deep breath of the sterile, processed air.

00:00.

The screen went black. Then the cherry blossom video resumed, serene and endless. The lights came up. The climate control hummed back to life. The door to Pod 17 gave a soft, unlocking chirp.

Manny did not move. He sat in the false comfort of the pod, the map burning behind his eyes. The failsafe was gone. The choice was his.

He was no longer a rat in the walls.

He was a spark in the server room.

He reached out and pushed the door open. It slid aside without resistance. The hallway was empty, glowing with its soothing green light. To his left was the way back to the lobby, to provisional safety, to eventual capture. To his right, deeper into the wellness wing, was a grey, unmarked door beside a potted fern. The service entrance.

He could feel the building around him, a living entity of data and light. It knew he was here. It was processing his next move, calculating probabilities. It would not calculate this. It could not comprehend a choice made not for survival, but for sacrifice.

Manny stepped out of the pod. He didn’t shuffle. He walked, his footsteps silent on the plush carpet, towards the grey door. He was a dead man walking. A biometric key. A message finally understood.

He reached the door. There was no handle. A keypad glowed faintly. He punched in the code the failsafe had given him. 767426#.

A click. The door swung inward, revealing a concrete stairwell lit by harsh fluorescent bars. The smell of dust, electricity, and cold concrete washed out. The sound of the building changed—from a whisper to a deep, mechanical thrum. The heartbeat of the system.

He stepped through, pulling the door shut behind him. The lock engaged with a final, metallic sound.

In the serene pod behind him, the touchscreen flickered. The cherry blossoms froze. For a lasting, permanent moment, the stark green text returned, a final epitaph from the ghost in the machine.

PROTOCOL UMBILICUS: ENGAGED. GODSPEED, LIVING KEY.

Then the screen went dark, and stayed dark.

Chapter 20

CHAPTER 20

The stairwell was a throat. The fluorescent bars buzzed like trapped flies, casting a sickly, vibrating light on the painted cinderblock. The thrum was a physical thing here, a pressure in Manny’s teeth, a vibration through the soles of his stolen sneakers. He descended, each step an echo swallowed by the hum. Service Level 2 read the stenciled letters on the door he passed. Service Level 3.

He was below the soil now. Below the gardens, the polished floors, the curated air. This was the root system. The code had been precise: Sub-Basement 2. Mechanical Nexus. Primary Data Conduit.

The door to Sub-Basement 2 was a slab of grey steel, featureless but for a card reader and a small, dark lens. No keypad. Manny stood before it, a dry taste in his mouth. The failsafe’s last gift. He was the key. He leaned in, let the lens capture his face. He felt nothing—no scan, no light. Just a moment of silent assessment.

The lock gave a heavy, oiled clunk. The door sighed open a half-inch, releasing a wave of heat and a deeper, more complex roar—the sound of water moving under pressure, of servers thinking, of turbines spinning. He pushed it open and stepped into the cathedral of the machine.

It was vast. A space the size of the community center gymnasium, but choked, layered, dense. Conduit pipes as thick as his torso ran along the ceiling in tangled bundles, sheathed in gleaming silver insulation. Below them, rows of server racks hummed, their indicator lights a constellation of cold blue and steady green.
